Even Respect for the Dead Fails to
Restrain a French Socialist.
PARIS, Feb. 20. â In the Chamber of
Deputies to-day the Premier, M. D ipjy,
isked for a credit of 160,000 franca to
Ipfray the expenses of the obsequi'-s of
â :he late President Faure.
Jf.' Jeante, Socialist, asked that the
>bsequies be purely civil.
This drew forth murmurs and pro
jests from the Rightists and Centrists.
Jho threatened to leave the chamber,
/he President of the Chamber of Depu-
JÂ«5, M. Deschanel, interrupted M. de
t^ante several times, calling upon him
io respect the stricken family of the
leceased.
M. de Jeante's motion was rejected
by "a vote of 444 to 68. and the credit
* as adopted by a vote of 463 to 42.
The Chamber unanimously decided to
mend the departure of the body from
!he Elysee Palace on Thursday, al
though the procession will really only
Â»c formed after leaving the Cathedral
it Notre Dame. A large multitude de
iled before the coffin of the dead Presi
lent to-day.
In view of the decision of the Cham-
W of Deputies it has now been ar
The San Francisco Call.
ranged that the entire funeral cortege
shall form at the Elysee Palace in
stead of at the Cathedral of Notre
Dame, as was previously intended.
Considerable comment has been
caused by the fact that a priest was
summoned to M. Faure'B bedside, al
though the President was a staunch
Free Mason. M. de Jeante, Socialist,
raised the subject to-day in the Cham
ber of Deputies. M. Legall, the Presi
dent's private secretary, when ques
tioned, declared that M. Faure, while

The strictest measures have been
taken to maintain order in the streets
to-night, the police, among other
things, having be*-n instructed to ar
rest immediately any person uttering
cries hostile to President Loubet.
According to the best authority,
President L,oubet'a message to Parlia
ment will not be a lengthy document.
After returning thanks for his election,
affirming his respect for the constitu
tion and pledging himself to uphold It
faithfully, both in spirit and letter the
President will appeal warmly for paci
fication and concord, and will eulogize
the army and express the hope that the
country will, in its attachment to the
army and its respect for justice, speed
ily witness the restoration of harmony
and the solution of questions that are
agitating the nation.
The President will then recall the
fact that he has always devoted him
self to the study of labor and social
questions, industrial matters^, financial
economy and the organization of indus
try, and he will insist upon Parliament
dealing with these questions in a faith
ful and practical manner.
Regarding foreign affairs, the mess
age vill say that In her relations with
toie} n nations France will pursue a
polic., which will remain faithful to
friendships formed, and that by its cor
rectness and disinterestedness will con
form to the traditions of her history.
This policy will be the surest guarantee
of peace and best calculated to main
tain the place which France now occu
pies in the world.

Epidemic of Burglaries.
WOODLAND, Feb. 20.â An epidemic of
burglaries seems to have broken out in
Yolo CÂ»unty. They are reported from all
parts of the county, and in most instances
the character of the Jobs indicate that
they are the work of novices, who. under
ordinary circumstances, would not com
mit a criminal act. There never was a
time whet more people were out of em
ployment In this county than at present.

HAWLEY'S MURDER IS A
TRAGEDY OF TWO LIVES
Alma E. Burton Tells a Pathetic
Story of Her Effort to Re=
deem a Drunkard,
Sad End to a Romance of the Slumsâ Paul Mamogni
Saw the Crime and Says He Can Identify
the Murderer.
In his downfall and death Charles M.
Hauley dragged Miss Alma E. Burton
to dishonor. The man was murdered,
and his murder has been quickly fol
lowed by the expose of the woman's
disgrace! This latest story of a local
crime is of unusual pathos. Hawley
gave up family, friends, position and
life for his inordinate love of drink.
Miss Burton has sacrificed her name
and brought shame to her family
through her devotion to a self-imposed
duty to go into the dregs of society and
among its outcasts to rescue those who
had fallen from drink.
Hawley received his death blow on
Sunday night on Grant avenue, near
Geary street. He lingered, without re
gaining consciousness, until 1:30 o'clock
yesterday afternoon, when he died. It
was at first supposed that Hawley
might have met his death by accident..
This theory has been destroyed, how
ever, by the facts. Four people, a man
and three boys, saw Hawley killed, and
â¢the description of the murderer, as giv
en by one of the witnesses, is sufficient
to afford the police a clew strong
enough upon which to. work.
The most important of the four wit
nesses is Paul Mamognl, who stood
within ten fet of Hawley when the
blow was struck. It was almost 9
o'clock in the evening when Hawley
walked, slightly staggering, up Grant
avenue from Geary. Not noticing
where he was going Hawley jostled
into a young fellow coming toward
him. The stranger pushed Hawley
away. The assailed man reeled toward
the curb, then stood smiling in maud
lin good nature at his assailant. The
stranger rushed toward him and struck
him viciously behind the neck. Haw
ley tumbled face down upon the stone
walk and the stranger ran down Grant
avenue to Geary where he disappeared
from the view of those who had seen
the murder. This is the scene, as Paul
Mamogni describes it.
Mamogni says that he feels confident
that he could identify the murderer if
he ever saw him again. The stranger,
Mamogni says, was shorter than Haw
ley, slight of build, smooth shaven,
light complexioned and light haired.
He wore a dark sack suit of clothes and
a dark derby hat. Mamogni, who is
employed by business men in the down
town district and is familiar with faces
and scenes of the tenderloin, feels sure
that he has seen the murderer before
as one of the depraved creatures who
haunt the resorts of Grant avenue after
nightfall.
The testimony of three boys, Fred
Racik, Patrick Dougherty and George
Doran, may be added to that of Ma
mogni and then the evidence thus far
secured to reveal the perpetrator of the
crime is complete. The three boys were
on Grant avenue within a few feet from
where Hawley received his death blow.
They saw the encounter, heard the
murderer apply a vile epithet to his vic
tim and then strike at him viciously.
They agreed with Mamognl that the
assailant was a comparatively small
man, light of feature and of hair and
dressed In black.
The police have discovered nothing
more with which to expose the mur
derer and less with which to explain
a motive for the crime. The strange
relations between the murdered man
and his contract wife, Miss Alma E.
Burtc/n, may give an explanation per
haps in the jealousy of some besotted,
degraded outcast, who took murderous
revenge for the attentions that were
given to another and not to him. A
chance encounter may clear away the
mystery, but it is to the relationship
between Hawley and Miss Burton that
tragic Interest attaches.
The story of this association as it was
revealed yesterday in the gloomy re
sorts of outcasts, in tenderloin lodging
houses, Salvation Army headquarters
and the offices of the police is of great
sadness. Hawley went from a place
of honor and trust into the dregs.
Three years ago he was a prosperous
clerk of recognized ability and excel
lent family connections. Gradually he
began to drink and occasionally to be
unreliable in his duties to his employ*
ers. His lapses became more frequent
until they ended in his dismissal. He
Htill had friends and credit won by lav
ish generosity on "the line." It was
not long before friends and credit dis
appeared and he found his last check
before suicide in the wretched home of
the Salvation Army at Salt Lake City.
He was all but nominally a drunken
pauper existing on the charity of a be
neficent organization that tries to keep
body and soul together in an effort to
redeem both.
Even the officers of the Salvation
Army at Salt Lake could do no more
with Hawley, but they were not ready
to abandon him and sent him practical
ly as a ward to the Salvation Army
of this city. He came here a physical
and mental wreck, despondent and dis
couraged, in the fall of 1897. The offi
cers of the Workingmen's Institute at
158 New Montgomery street took him
in, fed him and housed him. They
have but poor fare and lodging:, but to
many it is better than the humiliation
of pauperism.
Hawley looked for work, but could
not find it. He was sent to the Salva
tion Army farm in Alameda and re
turned. In his efforts to recover him
self to sobriety he frequented the Peniel
Mission on Montgomery avenue and
Kearny street. This mission is con
ducted by a local branch of a national
society for the single purpose of re
deeming drunkards and of aiding them
to regain t-heir feet in their struggle.
At the local mission Hawley met Miss
Alma E. Burton, one of the directing
spirits of the place.
Miss Burton is a woman with a mis
sion. She lives with her widowed
mother and brothers at 225 Fifth ave
nue in this city. Miss Burton believed
that her duty called her into the dregs
of the Barbary Coast, among the be
sotted, degraded beings who haunt the
alleys in search of liquor. Miss Burton
believed that some of these outcasts
might be redeemed and she went among
them to counsel with them, advise them
and suggest to them an avenue through
which they could return to decent liv
ing. She began one of the two man
agers of the mission.
A few months ago she met Hawley,
who, she says. Impressed her as a man
of more than ordinary intelligence and
Continued on Third Page.

MENTO, Feb. 20.â The claim put for

ceived to-day:
LEMON, Cal., Feb. 20, 1899.
Senator K. N. Bulla, Sacramento: Am

you for Senator. A. T. CURRIER.
It seems to be the opinion that Sena
tor Currier will not return to Sacra
mento unless the session is unduly pro
longed by reason of the Senatorial
strife.
Senator Simpson passed two hours to
day in writing explanatory letters to
his constituents. According to all ac
counts the men in the south who are
bracing up Simpson are a queer crowd.
One of the letters in the Record-Union
purports to be from John S. Cox of
Pasadena. Cox is not a resident of
Pasadena, but lives in Los Angeles.
Moreover, he is a "'bloomin' Britisher,"
who cannot vote because he is not nat
uralized. Another letter is from Frank
Dominguez, a half Mexican. Within
the last ten days Senator Bulla received
a letter from Dominguez in which the
latter pledged his fidelity to the Sena
tor.
Irving M. Scott arrived from San
Francisco this evening and reopened
hip headquarters in the Golden Eaerlp
Hotel. Shortly after his arrival he was
greeted by his supporters â Senators
Davis and Assemblyman Dunlap. Mr.
Scott comes to stay until the deadlock
is broken or the Legislature adjourns.
His friends advised him of the situa
tion here, explaining that Burns was
the only Senatorial candidate in the
field who had refused to sacrifice his
own candidacy to secure the election of
a Republican United States Senator. It
was shown that Grant, Barnes and
Buila were willing to make any sacri
fice demanded in order that the field
might be cleared for the election of a
Senator.
When the situation is carefully stud
ied with reference to the future of the
Republican party in this State, it is ad
mitted by leading Republicans that it
would be better for the Legislature to
adjourn without electing a Senator
than to elect Dan Burns. Failing to
elect, the party could go before the
people two years hence and say that
the orders of the machine and railroad
were resisted by the manly and loyal
Republicans of the Legislature, and
the people would commend the act.
Should Burns, however, be chosen
Senator, the party would be disrupted.
A Democratic Legislature would be thr
result and the apportionment for ten
years on Democratic lines would leave
the Republicans at the mercy of their
political adversaries. An adjournment
without electing Burns would be a no
tice served on Burns and the machine
that the Republican party respected
the will of the people and would be
governed by the voice of the people.
On account of sickness General
Barnes remained at the Sutter Club for
a few hours one day last week. The
touts, observing his absence, spread a
report that he had closed headquarters
and given up the contest. His support
ers say that if there is any candidate
in this contest prepared to stay to the
finish of the session and accept the re
sult whatever it may be that candidate
is General Barnes. They also say that
there is not the color of truth in the
rumor that he intends to draw out, but
there is to-night an exceedingly bright
propect that he will win out.
Facts are now coming to light re
garding the secret effort in San Joaquln
County to have the county committee
direct Assemblyman Muenter to sup
port the Dan Burns caucus scheme. It
appears that the Republicans of Stock
ton were not advised of the meeting oi
informed that a petition was in circu
lation asking Assemblymen Dunlap and
Muenter to get In and do the work of
the touts. It is a well-known fact that
the Republicans of San Joaquin are op
posed to Burns and do not want the
Legislature to elect him to the United
States Senate, yet there is an effort In
secret to set aside the will of the peo
ple and practically instruct the San
Joaquln delegation to vote for Burns.
Mr. Muenter and Mr. Dunlap are well
advised as to the sentiments of their
constituents, and they are altogether
too bright and enterprising to be de
ceived by any one-sided petition circu
lated in secret by the agents of the
"organization." .
The touts are endeavoring to work
the Simpson game on another member
of the Legislature. The game was
overplayed Sunday night and the spec*
tacle observed by many members of the
joint convention to-day did not call out
compliments for the tactics of the
touts. The thought of debasing a man
with liquor and low associations until
his moral sense Is so blunted that he
cannot distinguish right from wrong is
' so repulsive to high minded men in the
Legislature that steps may be taken
to denounce on the floor of the joint
convention the latest and lowest
method of controlling votes for the of
fice of United States Senator.
The forces of Burns continue the as
sault on Grant's column, but the San
Diego candidate seems undisturbed.
He does not hear of any defections.
Many of the rumors of contemplated
desertion have been investigated and
found to be false. There are no indi
cations that Burns is making headway
In his efforts to secure a caucus and
it is by no means certain that he can
win with a caucus, but there is an ab
solute certainty that he cannot win
without one. The Burns plan of cam
PRICE FIVE CENTS.
paign te clearly unfolded. He desires
first of all to break the Grant camp
and scatter the forces of that fold ta
various candidates possessing local fa
vor and in the end gather in for his
own support the scattered members.
Grant is fully and promptly advised of
every step taken by the Candelaria
candidate. There is an apprehension
that Burns in his desperation will re
sort to open bribery, hence a remarka
bly close watch is maintained in this
respect.
There is a strong undercurrent In
General Barnes' favor, and it is a sig
nificant fact that the General has ad
vanced in the pools until he ranks next
to Burns. It wo'-ld be no surprise to
well informed men if he should be in
the lead before the week ends. He is
known to be the second choice of a
large number of the members of the
Legislature. The unfair report circu
lated last week that he intended to stay
in San Francisco when he went home
for his Sunday visit has not injured his
cause.
The deadlock could be broken to
morrow if Burns would consent to the
| election of any one but himself. Other
candidates are willing to sacrifice their
personal preferences and ambitions to
preserve the party unity and secure
I the election of a Senator, but Burns
alone stands out. declarlner that he
must be vindicated, and that if he can
not be Senator no one else Bhall be.
Judge Dibble is not satisfied with his
prerogative of cracking the whip over
the Assembly, but is ambitious to ex
tend his leadership over the party. He
clings to the doctrine of Burns for Sen
ator or nobody. Members of the As
sembly may conclude to take the man
agement of legislative affairs out of the

AN EARLY CLOSE OF THE
LEGISLATURE AUGURED
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO. Feb. 20.-Assemblyman Valen
tine of Los Angeles, who is chairman of
the Ways and Means Committee, expects
to have the general appropriation bill on
the floor of the Assembly to-morrow. If
he carries out his intention it augurs an
early finish to this session. As a general
thing the promulgation of the general ap
priation bill is the last and most important
task that falls to the lot of the Ways and
Means Committee and it is Invariably the
precursor of adjournment sine die.
Apropos of adjournment to-day was the
last day of the session on which bills
might be introduced, but even so there
was scarcely more than a dozen and few
of them such as one experienced of
Legislatures expects to see rushed In at
the eleventh hour. The "cinch bill" of
dishonored history was not numerously
among them.
Crowder of San Diego was the author of
two of them. One of them establishes the
maximum rate for railroad transportation
at 3 cents per mile first-class and 2' 2 cents
per mile second-class. It also makes the
rate for children under 12 years of ago
*Â± cent per mile, and requires that first
and second class tickets shall be sold at
every station and for all trains.
His othr>r bill provides a whole section
for the traveler who has bought but one
$2 50 berth in a sleeper. It requires that
when only the lower berth of a sootion
is sold the upper berth must not be let
down until it, too. Is disposed of. As it
Is, it Is the delight of the Ã¼biquitous por
ter to rush frantically down the car and
with a rattle of chains let down thf up
per berth just after the man who has not
left up the usual dollar has gotten com
fortably asleep. Mr. Crowder proposes to
Bleep without paying an aggregate of $3 50
for his bed when tie goes back to San
Diego from this Legislature, and he as
signs no other reason for his proposed en
actment.
Mr. Caminetti's anti-treat bill passed
with little in the way of opposition. It
provides that no candidate for office shall
liquefy his campaign with any intoxicat
ing or temperance drinks. He shall not
buy for nor give to any one. whether for
his vote or not, any beverage of any de
scription.
It was strongly backed by Grove L.
Johnson, who told the history of his life
and his record on prohibition. It was also
backed by Judge Dibble, who said he had
not spent a cent in riotous living during
the last election because he had been siok
in bed.
Lawrence Hoey of San Francisco op
posed the measure on the broad principle
that it was so general and withal vague
in its provisions that it could be easily
evaded. He said. too. that he thought It
was his good American privilege to treat
when he could afford it and he denied any
man's right to curtail it.
Assemblyman Raw of El Dorado fought
the bill when it came up in 1893 and he
fought it again to-day. He ridiculed what
he called an attempt to infringe upon the
personal rights of an American citizen,
and spoke forcefully against the meas
ure and its general tenor. Brown of San
Mateo added his voice to the chorus
against the measure, but it was all of no
avail, the vote showed few supporters
for the opposition and the measure car
ried.
Senator Dickinson's Senate bill 59.
which provides for the "inspection of
dairies and factories for the making of
dairy products and of dairy products as
to the sanitary condition and as to the
health of stock and to prevent the sale of
milk drawn from diseased animals." was
passed after several speeches of indorse
ment had been made in its favor.
Atherton of Marin spoke strongly for it.
Le Barron of Sonoma said his " people
strongly Indorsed it. and all of the other
Cow County Representatives swelled the
chorus.

A RAILROAD BILL
WITH A JOKER
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, Fek 2').â Assemblyman Valen
tine of Los Angeles Introduced in the As
sembly to-<lay a bill which doubtless
every railroad in the State will take
pleasure in becking. It amends section
456 of the. < ivil Code wo that railroad cor
porations may mortgage and remortgage
their properties so long as any one can
be found to loan money on successive
bond issues. It throws off the present
limitation that bonds may be issued only
to the extent of a railroad's capitaliza
tion and makes it possible for any road
or combination of roads to keep transpor
tation charges up to the top notch in spite
of the Railroad Commission. It aceom
lilishÂ«Â»s all this by permitting; ii company
to mortgage and remortgage, the accru
ing interest to stand as a fixed charge
a.gainst the company. As the Railroad
Commission cannot reduce fares to a rate
so low that the interest pro rata cannot
y<G met, the little joker in Mr. Valentine's
bill is seemingly apparent.
Section 456 provides for the borrowing
of money, and to it, before Valentine's
bill cut it out, was attached the follow
ing clause:
"The amount of bonds or promlesory
hptes issued for such purposes must not
exceed in all the amount of the capital
stock."
That is the little joker in the. bill. The
section is allowed to remain as ft was
with the exception that this clause is left
out. Mr. Valentine says he introduced tho
bill by request and is now looking into it.
He says that If the bill proves to be as
it appears on its face he will withdraw It
or allow it to die peacefully and quietly
in committee.
Ready to Remove San Quentin.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, Feb. 20 âThe- delegation of the
Assembly Committee on Prisons and
Reformatories returned this morning
from Its junketing tour to San Quenttn.
where It went to inspect the building." of
the State Prison. In regard to which
there is a bill before the Assembly. Those
of the committee who went were: Assem
blymen Bliss, Atherton, Falrweather,
Eugene Sullivan. Henry and Glenn, 1..a
bill provides for the removal of a portion
of the prison. If not all, from its present j
locality. It appears to be the desire of the â¢
people of Marfn County to have it as far
away as possible, as they claim that at !
present it is a decided menace to the best
interests of the county. It is claimed
that by allowing six years for the re
moval there would be sufficient time for
the selling of the present buildings and
site to advantage and in that way de
rive enough money to build a prison,
which will accommodate the less des
perate cases, while the others could be'
transferred to Folsom. The committee
found the means for accommodations at â¢
San Quentin very inadequate and appear ,
to be heartily in favor of the bill.
A MOVE MADE
IN THE DARK
A Little Quiet "Instructing"
Done for Burns at
Stockton.
STOCKTON, Feb. 20.â The represent
atives nf Dan Burns in the executive
committee of the Republican County
Committee got in their work to-day, i
This executive committee has become |
famous for indorsing any old thing on
the quiet. The first move in favor of
the Mexican was completed to-day
when the following resolutions were
'circulated' quietly among some of the
members who were "on":
Whereas, Recognizing the fact that the
material intercuts of the State of Califor
nia may be jeopardized should there be a ;
failure by the Legislature in session to ;
elect a Republican United States Senator
to succeed Hon. Stephen M. White, whose
term expires on the 4th of March next,
and realizing; that the existing deadlock
ia causing a feeling- of uneasiness in the :
Republican party, throughout the State,
and believing it to be the duty of the
members of the Legislature *o break the
deadlock, and to use all honorable means j
to secure the election of such Senator; i
therefore be it
Resolved, By the executive committee of j
the Republican Uounty Central Commit
tee of San Joaquln County, that our As
semblymen from this county be and they !
are hereby urgently requested to assist
or if necessary to take the initiative in |
such proceedings, caucuses or such other
means as may be necessary and proper to j
break the deadlock and assure the elec- |
tkm of a Republican United States Sena- !
tor at this session of the Legislature.
These were signed by a number of !
the city committeemen. The purpose !
of these? "instructions" are to make it
easy for Assemblymen Dunlap and
Muenter to go into caucus, and should
Burns be the nominee they could say
i they merely followed instructions. Re
publicans regard the work of Chair
man Harkness and his few friends as
nothing short of political perfidy. They
dared rot call the Republican County
Committee together, as anything the
least favorable to Burns would have
| been almost unanimously voted down.
This is not the first time this "execu
tive committee" has made a wild move
like this, but it will probably be the
last.
When interviewed Dr. George 8.
Harknesa, chairman of the committee,
said:
"It is very important that a Senator
should be chosen. If the present Leg
islature does not make a selection and
leaves matters in an unsettled condi
tion the next Legislature will probably
be Democratic and we will then lose a
Senator. We can never tell how im
portant one vote in Congress may bÂ«.
Wry ofton grave questions hinge on
one vote. I think that some arrange
ments shouid be made whereby a Sena
tor will be elected."

IMPORTANT MEASURES
IN THE ASSEMBLY
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, Feb. Grove L. Johnson of
Sacramento this morning introduced In
the Assembly a measure which is de
signed to take the place of Assembly bill
2, appropriating $15,000 for a sewerage sys
tem at Folsom State prison, which was
vetoed by the Governor on Saturday.
Mr. Johnson's new bill makes an ap
propriation of the same amount as the
old bill, but it further provides that all
plans and specifications shall be sanc
tioned by a majority of the Board of
Prison Directors and the State Board of
Examiners. No payment for work done
under the bill shall be made until such
payment has been authorized by the
Board of Examiners and no plan for the
construction of the proposed system shall
be approved by the Board 'of Examiners
if the same is in excess of the sum of
$15,000. ; -
The bill was referred to the Committee
on Ways and Means.
Works of San Diego introduced by re
quest a hill which makes an appropria
tion of $5000 for the addition to the "well"
in the Capitol building of a restaurant,
barber shop and baths. His bill went to
the Committee on Public Works.
Assembly bill 941, offered this morning
by Dibble, so amends section 165 of the
Penal Code that any person who gives or
offers a bribe to any member of any
Board of Trustees, Board of Education
or Board of School Directors shall be
guilty of a felony, and any such director
or trustee who shall accept such bribe is
also guilty of a felony and punishable by
imprisonment for not more than fourteen
years nor less than one and is disquali
fied from again holding public office in
the State. â¢ ' " , , ,
As the law is at present the acquisitive
school director is Immune from the oper
ation of the laws regulating bribery, and
in order that the authorities in San Fran
cisco may have some surcease from the
perennial crookedness of its school boards
the District Attorney's office has draft
ed the new bill and asked Judge Dibble to
present It. It Is in the hands of the Ju
diciary Committee.

Trying to Expedite Business.
(ALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO. Feb. 20.â The Committee on
Rules, which has been considering the
best way of expediting the work of the
Senate, reported these recommendations:
That the special urgency file be taken up
each evening: at 8 o'clock and that its
consideration be continued until adjourn
ment; that the Senate third reading file
be. at other times not in conflict with any
other rule, considered until finally dis
posed of; that from time to time as the
consideration of the bills on the Senate
emergency file shall have been practically
completed, that new files be made up ac
cording to the alphabetical rollcall of the
Senators present: that any matter dis
posed of upon the second reading of any
bill shall not again bo presented for the
consideration of the Senate at the third
reading of the same without the suspen
sion of the rules.
The report was made a special order for
to-morrow morning.

GRANT'S FORCES
TO REMAIN SOLID
CALL, HEADQUARTERS. SACRAMENTO. Feb. 20.â After talking
in a general way all afternoon twenty-two of the twenty-seven support
ers of U. S. Grant got tcrgether to-night and renewed their allegiance to
the San Diego candidate. The caucus was held in room 58 of the State
Capitol. The absentees were Assemblymen Blood, Clark, Dale, Cargill
and Marvin. The three first mentioned were ' vouched for as men who
could be relied upon to stand out for all time against the approaches of
the Burns element or the wiles of any other candidate opposed to the
election of Grant. Cargill and Marvin were not vouched for with that
absence of reserve which marked the pledges for the others, but it wrvs
s.u J that they would not waver in their Riipport of Grant.
The twenty-two members present resolvcu to stand firm this week.
Senator Smith, the chairman of the caucus, was. however, authorized
to confer with all candidates other than Burns and invite a conference
to convene next Wednesday evening at 7:30 o'clock. This proposition
was thp subject of prolonged and animated discussion. It was suggested
and decided that if thi3 conference should be well attended, and all
should agree tn support one candidate, then the whole strength should be
cast for, that candidate. Should the conference appointed for Wednes
day fall" to agree on a candidate or fail to convene by reason of sparse
attendance the Grant forces are to continue to present a solid front
and in no event entertain a proposition to enter a caucus proposed in the
interest of Burns.

WORK IN THE SENATE
NOT VERY BRISK
CAT.T, HEADQUARTERS, BACRA
MENTO, Feb. 20.â Senator <"utter sub
mitted an amendment to tho constitution
by which it is provided that special and
local laws may be passed by a vote of
four-flfths of each house of the Legisla
ture.
A petition from five hundred residents
of San Diego, submitted by Senator Nutt,
asking for the repeal of the law com
pelling school children to be vaccinated,
was ordered printed in the Journal.
Senator Bufla submitted a bill provid
ing for a building and loan commission
of two members appointed by the Gov
ernor, one to be known as the super
visor, at a salary of $2400 a year, and the
other as the Inspector at a salary of $2100
a year. The supervision of the building
and loan societies is made their duty, and
a fine of $500 is provided in case they do
not report the approaching insolvency of
any society.
Senator Burnett submitted a bill com
pelling the affixing of the names and ad
dresses of the printer of every piece of
campaign printing.
Senator Dickinson submitted a bill al
lowing the transfer during 1599 and 1900 of
money from other funds into the general
fund 'in order to replenish the general
fund, such money, however, to be paid
back as soon as possible.
It was the last day for the submission
of bills without special permission of the
Senate, and It was expected there would
be a flood of them, but such was not the
case.
VOTING MACHINE BILL
IS ON THE SENATE FILE
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, Feb. 20.â A bill providing for the
establishment of voting machines in the
various counties of the State was intro
duced In the Senate by Senator Smith to
day In his measure Senator Smith pro
vides that Boards of Supervisors and
Boards of Trustees may call a meeting, at
which they may provide for the use of
ballot machines. These, it Is set forth,
may be used at general elections, but
need not be used at elections of school
officers at which no other officers are
voted for.
It is required that none but machines
which maintain absolute secrecy and
which keep an accurate and concealed
register of the results of the vote shall be
used.
When a city or county, according to the
bill, shall determine on the use of ma
chines the governing board shall formu
late rules governing elections, which must
be submitted to the Attorney General for
his approval at least ninety days pre
ceding the election to be so held.
It is further provided that in cities
using ballot machines there shall be no
ballot clerks and but three Judges to each
precinct. All the laws applicable to bal
loting machines are continued in force
by the terms of the measure, and those
in conflict with suoh elections 1 are sus
pended In the counties where machines
are used.
San Pedro's Harbor.
CALL, HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, Feb. 20.â The Assembly Commit
tee on Commerce and Navigation returned
this morning from San Pedro, where it
went to inspect the harbor with the view
of reporting upon the bill which provides
for the establishment of a harbor com
mission there. At San Pedro the commit
tee was met by two different factions, one
in favor of the establishment of a harbor
commission and one in opposition to it.
The committee was divided in Its opinion
upon the matter, but the majority seemed
to be unfavorable to the establishment of
the harbor commission, and that will most
likely to be the substance of the report at
the next meeting.
IN WHITE UNIFORMS.
A Beneficial Change to the City's
Street-Sweeping Force,
The members of the Merchants' Asso
ciation were highly pleased yesterday by
the appearance on the streets down town
of a lot of street sweepers dressed in
white garments and wearing white hats.
When the work of cleaning the thorough
fares by hand first came into vogue the
officers of the association had their men
dress in brown goods. In time they
learned that the wnite goods was better
for several reasons. The principal one
was that the men were less liable to be
run down by drivers, the white goods be
ing easily seen. Wh^n Mr. Kingwent East
several months ago he found in nearly all
of the big cities that this style of gar
ment prevailed and even in the little
towns where sweeping was done by hand.
Upon hts return he advised that the
change be made.and the association In as
sisting the Supervisors in drawing up the
contract for street sweeping specified that
the sweepers should be dressed in white
duck. The men themselves are pleased
with the change, as they realize that the
uniform really makes them a part of the
city's working force.

MAILDR!VERS CLAMOR
FOR THEIR SALARIES
EECEIVE NO COMPENSATION TOR
THREE MONTHS.
Blame Sub-Contractor Morrison for
the Delay and Are Busy
Standing Off Land
lords.
Through the carelessness or incompe
tency of a local contractor named Morri
son six drivers of Uncle Sam's mail have
been without their hard-earned salaries
for nearly three months. The amount
due the sextet of drivers is not overlarge,
as they earn but $30 and under a month,
out of which they clothe, feed and lodge
themselves.
The withholding of their money has
worked a hardship on the men, as. their
boarding house keepers have been clam
oring for money, and the men can only
answer that some is due them and may
be received any day. These empty prom
ises so long made have become monoto
nous to 'the landlords, who have them
selves tried to make collections, but with
the same results.
The men alluded to are Foreman Jack
Phelan, Jack Davis, M. W. Hamilton.
William Dletz, "Pop" Morrison and a boy
named "Joe." They were employed by
Morrison, who. it seems, is an agent for
the Midland Mall Company of Clinton,
Mo. The men are perfectly contented to
arise each morning: at 4:30, be at their
stations an hour later and work until late
In the evening, but they do object to la
boring for glory and living on air, and
therefore rebel. On the first of the pres
ent month they had mutually arranged
to quit work in a body, but cooler counsel
prevailed and they changed their plan.
Morrison nas been appealed to time
and again by the workers, but they have
been put off as often as they asked. The
sub-contractor is lying in bed at his
home at 516 Folsom street, suffering from
illness, and the drivers have been unable
to see him during the past few weeks. A
week ago they received renewed assur
ances of receiving their pay from J. N.
Games, a representative of the Midland
Mall Company. The latter explained to
the men that Morrison had either through
ignorance or mistake improperly made
out the official documents, causing a delay
in the payment of their salary. Mr.
Games is staying at the New Western
Hotel, and to one of the drivers, who, ac
companied by his boarding-house keeper,
called on him last evening, he said the
money would shortly be paid.
Mr. Games explained the present
trouble in the following manner: "The
president of the Midland Mail Company,
J. P. Stewart, and W. H. Smith bid and
were awarded the contract for carrying
the mail to and from the trains in this
city. Mr. Stewart delegated to Mr. Mor
rison the duty of employing the drivers
and furnishing the horses. The latter was
also authorized to pay the men with
money received from Stewart. This plan
went along all right until the latter part
of October, when Stewart failed. New
contracts, in which Stewart was inter
ested, were sublet and Morrison became
a sub-contractor, but in filling out his
papers he made many mistakes and they
were returned from Washington. Owing
to the sickness of Morrison they were un
corrected until I arrived two weeks ago.
I tilled the papers out properly and for
warded them to Washington. There has
not been time for their examination and
I therefore do not expect them returned
for another week." f
So. until they do arrive, the men will
not be compensated for their hard work.
Even "Pop" Morrison, the father of the
sub-contractor, "kicked" for his money,
and now that he learns the delay in its
payment was due to his son's procrastina
tion he will probably lecture him on his
ways.
As for the poor drivers, they live in
hope and stand off their landlords.
KILPATKICK IS THE WINNEB.
The School Principal Beinstated and
Gets His Back Salary.
The appeal case of E. C. Ivilpatrick
against the Board of Education was dis
missed yesterday by the Supreme Court.
Since the beginning of this year this mat
ter has really been a dead issue. It will
be remembered that over a year ago the
old Board of Education had a serious diffi
culty with the principal of the Business
Evening School over remarks that the
gentleman had made reflecting upon the
integrity of some of the members. The
board decided that there was but one
thing to do and that was to discipline the
too talkative Kilpatrlck, the supposition
being that if he were not corrected others
might also become free of speech and
state some facts that would not renect
credit if maae public. Mr. Kilpatrick
would not eat humble pie worth a cent,
and reiterated the offensive statements
that some members of the Board of Edu
cation had sold positions to teachers, and
tnat $500 was the ordinary price for a
place in the School Department.
i- or this he was dismissed, the charge
being generalâ unprofesional conduct. Kil
patrTck refused to accept this as final, and
began an action in the Superior Court to
secure his regular monthly pay and to
compel the board to reinstate him in his
old position. In the lower court Judge
Hunt decided in his favor on both points,
but the Board of Education was dissatis
fied and appealed the case to the higher
court. In the slow order of things there
the case finally oarne up, and by this time
the old board being out of office the new
board concluded that there was nothing
in maintaining the contest and consented
to a dismissal of the appeal, which really
is a victory for Kilpatnck, who will be re-
Instated and get his back salary for a
year or more.
WORK ON VALLEY ROAD.
Considerble progress is now being made
on the Valley road between Point Rich
mond and Stockton, and It will not be
long before the State will 15e reaping the
advantage of tho new line.
The work near Point Richmond is rap
idly advancing. A steam shovel is now
being operated in excavating 250,000 cubic
yards to fill in a portion of the bay for
about 3000 feet for the purpose of saving
time and distance.
The debris from the recent slide in the
big Franklin tunnel has been cleared
away and the tunnel toward Concord is
nearly finished.
The Bucknuan Contracting Company
says that the tunnel near Pinole will be
completed in three months. The work has
been delayed for scarcity of lumber and
other needed material, which the company
r.ow has In plenty.
The same company has a cut and riill
near Rodeo Creek and also th* building
of a concrete culvert. About 100,000 cubic
yards of rock will have to be moved, and
though it is a slow and laborious propo
sition, the contractors B*y it will be com- j
pleted in less than three months.

GHOULISH QUARREL
BREADS OUT ANEW
Strife of Los Angeles Uodertakers
Over the Body of
Hunter.
LOS ANGELES, Feb. 20.â The body of Attorney Jay E. Hunter, who
died as a result of the wounds inflicted by "W. H. Alford, was to-day re
moved lo the undertaking rooms uf iircsee Brothers at the request of
Hunter's friends. This rekindled the feud between Coroner Holland and
the undertaking fraternity. When Holland heard his enemies had pos
session of the body his anger was great. Earl Ropers, attorney for Al
ford, notified the Coroner that the body was decomposing and that an
immediate autopsy was necessary. This morning Holland served the
following notice on the Bresees:
"I hereby officially demand the body of Jay E. Hunter, deceased, for
the purpose "of holding an autopsy and inquest as prescribed by law."
The Coroner held another one of those famous conferences with the
District Attorney. General Jones told Holland that he felt some respect
was due the dead man, and that his remains should not be the subject
of a Coroner's quarrel, and he advised Coroner Holland to hold the in
quest. Holland gave in and held the inquest, at the suggestion of Gen
eral Jones, where the body lay.
"I held the autopsy and inquest by request and shall determine my
rights by law later on," said the Coroner to-night.

Catholic Ladies' Aid Society's Fair.
The Catholic Ladies' Aid Society will
hold a fair in Native Sons" Hall from
April 27 to May 6 inclusive. The proceeds
will be devoted to the replenishing of tho
treasuries of every branch of the society,
of which there are twelve in this city and
nine throughout the interior of California.
For the past ten years the lady members
have been working assiduously wherever
any assistance or relief could be admin
istered and during that period $75,755 has
been distributed among the poor, regard
loss of creed or color. The society is sole
ly sustained by dues collected from mem
bers.

ate chamber rang to the pent-up woes
of those who had felt the lash and
those who feared it, and then the pet
measure of young Mr. Works was
amended and re-amended and sent to
the printer to recuperate for another
...round on third reading.
.]â¢ It was the wildest time the orators
â¢ .have brad since the beginning of the

When the bill came up Senator Cur

Sec. 255. It shall be unlawful to pub
lish In any newspaper, handbill, poster,
book or serial publication, or supplement

of a person holding a public office in this

It shall likewise be unlawful to publish

IHE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, FEBRUARY 21, 1899.
Sims of Sonoma rose to oppose the bill.
"This Senate." he said, "should not
pass laws to restrict the pictorial ex
pressions of the opinions of the press.
Any one who has recognized the great
ness of this country has seen that much
| of its greatness has been due to a fear
-1 less expression of opinion on the part
\ of the press and to the free exercise of
I the right of free speech on the part of
I the people.
"The press has been the most power
! ful element of modern civilization; it
has contributed more to the history of
I the country than any other instrument,
| and its rights should not be curbed.
; There are libel laws to protect any .one
! who believes himself injured and they
i can be made to punish the publication
of anything untrue, and as we should
not suppress the expression of opinion in
| the columns so we should not legislate
I against the expression of opinion by
cartoons."
Senator Simpson was of a different
mind. "I had not thought," he said, "to
say anything on this subject, but if
there is any one who has the right to
stand up and declare himself for the
rights of the people, I am that man."
He then went on to justify himself for
going over to Burns. He believed there
should be a certain amount of liberty
I for the press, and he recognized the
right of the papers to criticize when a
j man had been guilty of malfeasance In
office or had betrayed a trust, but when
he had exercised an individual right
the Senator from Pasadena thought the
! papers had no business to interfere.
Senator Morehouse claimed the floor
when Senator Simpson had finished.
Now Morehouse can grow enthusiastic
on many subjects, but there is none
which wings his muse quicker or higher
i than the rights of the press. He has
felt the sting of both pen and pencil
and he speaks by the book â and he can
speak when he wants to. He had full
I swing for an hour in the afternoon and
along in the evening he remembered a
few things he might have said, but
which had slipped his memory, and so
he rose again and started another out
burst of as bitter and scathing a de
nunciation as was ever whirled around
his own head.
He declared there were many news
papermen whom he knew and some
I whom he loved, but there was one for
! whom he had the deepest, most undy
ing, unquenchable, unsparing, uncon
trollable hatred, and he declared he
would mention his name only it would
be a disrespect to the honorable body
of which he was a member.
"No newspaper has any more right
than an individual," he began, "and it
has no more right to blast a man's
character or reputation. We should
stand like men and let them know there
j is a limit beyond which no artist and
Ino scribbler can go. The point here in
) this bill and in the amendment is that
; no newspaper shall do a man an injury;
! when it expects to slander and blacken
| it should be stopped; all we ask is that
j it do no wrong."
Then the Senate took up the difficul
ties of securing redress through the
course prescribed by the law. â  He re
ferred to the farce of trying to secure a
summons on Hearst, the absent pro
prietor of the Examiner. He described
the cool and deliberate methods of the
courts and the callowness of some of
the District Attorneys, and declared
! that no one could get justice in this
; land where justice was the fundamental
principle.
"And who is the man who is re
sponsible?" the irate orator shouted in
tones that made the gallery shiver.
â¢Can you go to New York and kill the
vile dog? No â it is murder. You go to
the office in San Francisco and they
all hide behind the paper, and then
when you have them up here before a
Senate committee they refuse to answer
and go to jail until the Legislature is
over and has lost jurisdiction.
"No man appreciates newspaper men
more than I do, but here is this bill:
it allows them to publish the portrait
of a man when there is no malice and
no ill-will. It is enough. Is there any
man in this Senate so low as to Rive
the press power to ruin him? My mo
tives may not agree with those of Mr.
Hearst, for he does not know how to
run a paper, but with a certain un
namable man, to mention whose name
would be a disrespect to this Senate."
He referred to a cartoon in last Sun
; day's Call. Colonel Jackson was por-
I trayed in the act of drilling a hole
i through the door of a safe â breaking a
I deadlockâ and Major McLaughlin was
I eagerly watching in the distance the
i success of the preparation. "There was
no venom, no personality in the car
toon," said Morehouse, "and it is to
protect just such cartoons that this bill
i is here before us."
Senator Shortridge had no use for the
| bill or the amendments. He declared it
! was unconstitutional and would be
I found so by the Supreme Court if it
' should ever become a law, for, he said,
j it was a restriction of the liberty of
i the press guaranteed by the Constitu
! tion of the United States.
The right of free speech, said Senator
Shortridge. goes hand in hand with the
j liberty of the press. If you assail the
I one you strike at the other, and if you
I curb the press you abridge free speech.
He declared that the broad question of
the liberty of the press was the real one
j under discussion, and he demanded
j that the whole press should not suffer
I for the excesses of a small part.
Senator Boyce asked the difference
| between liberty and license, and an*
other half hour was devoted to the defi
nition of terms. Shortridge declared
that the printing of cartoons was one
! way of expressing opinions and came
within the limits of the constitutional
guarantee. If cartooning by pictures
j could be abridged, he said, so could car
tooning by words, and that the law
I would not allow. "The pen of the
writer and the cartoonist," he conclud
ed, "whips into line the rascal and the
rogue, and in doing so is a benefit to
j society. I have an appreciation for the
! whip when it is laid on the back of a
| rascal and I have a respect for the pen
; in an honest hand, and for us, as Sena
i tors, to abridge the liberty of either is
' flying in the face of the constitution."
Senator Gillette spoke in a similar
j vein. He believed the laws were broad
enough to cover the ground already.
i He believed if a man was caricatured.
! his own conduct was responsible and
j he should not complain.
Sims spoke again, coldly and logic
ally as before, and opposed the bill on
I no other ground than that of public
policy and constitutionality. Then
' Simpson tried another round, but he
I tried to merge a question of personal
I privilege into the discussion and he
was silenced with a point of order.
! The members did not wish to hear
again why he voted for Burns.
Senator Jones won for himself a
round of applause by saying cartoon
ing had gone so far it was neither
amusing nor damaging and, if let alone,
! it would die of itself.
Smith wanted the amendment
I downed, and said so with the brevity
which characterizes him, and then,
after more talk, the great question went
over to the night session.
Dickinson started the ball rolling in
the evening. He said much that was
! to the point, but in rounding un the
! absentee proprietor of the Examiner
i there was none so bitter as he. He
spoke of the difference between liberty
and license, and then picked to pieces
the morals of at least one man who
tries to show the path of right and
wrong. He told what he knew of
I Hearst and his manner of living at
j Sausalito. "I live in Sausallto," he
j said, in speaking of Hearst, "and no
i man on this floor who has a wife would
associate with him as I have seen him
on the boat going back and forth, to
and fro, between San Francisco and
Sausalito. And this is the man who
dares tell me what I should do and how
I should do it."
Then Morehouse was given another
chance and he duplicated his success of
the afternoon and then the amendment
went to a vote.
Leavltt's amendment adding the
word "ridicule" was adopted. Then
Curtin's amended amendment came up
and a roll call was demanded. The
amendment was carried by the follow
ing vote:
Ayesâ Ashe, Bettman, Boyce, Bulla,
Burnett, Chapman, Curtin, Cutter,
Dickinson, Feeney, Flint, Hall, Jones,
Leavitt, Morehouse, Simpson, Taylor,
Trout, Wolfeâ l9.
Xoesâ Braunhart, Davis. Doty, Dwy

Nutt. Pace, Prisk. Rowell, Shortridge,

Before the meeting adjourned Leavitt
made a motion that all the bills re
specting the liberty of the press be
made a special order for to-morrow
evening, but the Senate had had
enough of oratory and refused to con
sider the proposition.
The Works bill will come up once
more off the file for third reading, and
then it is predicted that the Senators,
having had their fling, will vote it down
and so end the matter.

MANAGER BRADY'S LOSS.
Receives the News of the Death of
His Son in New York.
William A. Brady, who managed the
six-day bicycle race in the Pavilion last '
week, received the Bad news yesterday
that his son. William A. Jr., aged 5Va
years, had died of pneumonia.
During the whole time the races were in
progress the boy lay sick at the home on
West One Hundred and Twenty-third
street. New York, and while being irri- I
tated with the thousand and one worries â 
consequent upon the management of the ;
cycling carnival, down at the bottom of !
his heart the fond father saw only the i
little sufferer on his bed of pain. All !
who came in contact with Mr. Brady were i
convinced that something was worrying â 
him; he was absent-minded and despond
ent at times and was even accused by !
some of having lost his skill as a man- j
agtT and mismanaging the race.
But a few of his most intimate friends
knew the real cause of the trouble. Every
two hours, day and night, a telegraphic
message was handed to Mr. Brady telling 'â 
of the condition of the boy he loved. The
dispatches were all encouraging in char
acter, but Mr. Brady more than suspected
thpy were of a deceptive nature; that they
hid the real truth to ease his mind and
this perhaps only added to his worry.
Now that the end has come many people
who wondered at the vague, unsatisfac
tory answers given by him to questions of
greatest import will understand. It was a ;
case of body here and a mind thousands ,
of miles away by the bedside of a little
scfferer.
As soon as he had received the sad news
Mr. Brady boarded the first train for the
East and is now speeding toward the
darkened, sorrowful home.

Messrs. Johnson and McCutcheon of

fellow members of the aristocratic golf
club which has its headquarters at San
Rafael and planking down some gold
pieces on Mr. Thomas' desk.
The visitors were perspiring-, lor the day
was warm and they had come in a hurry
to pay a license for selling- champagne
and other liquid truck in tneir olubrooms.
Mr. Thomas said that he could not re
ceive the money, and that all payments
should be made to Internal Revenue Col
lector Belrude ai Sacramento, but he
eased their fears by informing them they
did not need to feel any alarm, as they
had until the end of tho month in which
to pay the license.
Mr. Thomas declined to talk about the
matter except that the gentlemen repre
senting the club had offered to pay tho
money. They had been laboring under a
misapprehension. They were new in tho
business of selling liquor, he added; that
is, they had only just started up to dis
pense hospitality of the Kentucky kind in
the elubrooms, and they had until thÂ«
end of the month to pay, and that was
all that there was to it.

to pay an internal revenue tax if they
established a sideboard for members only,
and that he had advised them that they
were. His advice was disregarded and,
the sideboard was inaugurated without

"Jackies."
Carter de Haven, the clever little
sketch artist who Is adding further fame
to his name as an actor and dancer, has
won the hearts of the lowa's crew. His
graceful steps tickled the fancy of the
brave aggregation from the famous war

Orpheum in a body by encoring him vo
ciferously. -"..,_.â ,

jackets that they decided to show him
their appreciation in a more substantial
manner by presenting him with a neat lit
tle sailor suit to wear as a token of their
kindly feelings. The following letter ex
plains the proposed gift:
U. S. S. IOWA, San Francisco, Feb. IS.

C Moore, has kindly volunteered to call on you
to measure Carter for the sailor suit Mr.
Charrette anl I promised him last night as a
gilt and token of appreciation from the crew
of the battle ship lowa.

to take the measure and we will endeavor to
have the suit ready for Carter when he visits
the ship with you and his sister on Tuesday
afternoon? On behalf of the crew, and per
sonally I again venture to hope that we shall
see you and the children on board on the after
noon specified. "With regards and best wishes
from Mr. Charrette, myself and all the itpw
to the children and yourself. I remain, yours
sincerely. EUGENE M. ISAAC,

WRANGLE OF THE
SPANIARDS OVER
NATION'S DEFEAT
CouQt d'Almeoas Coroplains That
"Five Months Had Elapsed
and Not a Single Gen
eral Been Shot."
MADRID, Feb. 20,â The Cortes reas
sembled to-day. The galleries in both
chambers were thronged with an ex
pectant crowd. The Senate was very
full, hardly a single general being ab-
Bent,
Senor Montero Rios, president of the
Senate.. in opening the proceedings, pro
nounced.a eulogy upon M. Faure. and
a resolution of condolence with France
was adopted unanimously.
Senor Sagasta, the Premier, then pro
ceeded to refer the bill providing for
the cession of the Philippines to the
United States to a special committee,
but the Conservatives protested against
this, declaring that the bill ought to be
conscientiously discussed, and Senor
Sagasta withdrew his proposal.
. Count d'Almenas then brought up the
question of the conduct of the generals
engaged in the war in Cuba, declaring
that General Primo de Rivera, General
Weyler, General Blanco. Admiral Cer
vera and General Linares had proven
failures.
.This declaration elicited much ap
plause from the public galleries, in con
sequence of which several of the spec
tators wÂ« re expelled from the Chamber.
Observing th t he would deal with
the â shai.'f".' capitulation of Santi
ago," Count d'Almenas asked the House
whether he should proceed and was
answered with cries of "Yes" and "No,
End a general uproar ensued.
A repetition of the query provoking
still greater tumult Senor Sagasta rose
and defended the Government and its
peace commission. The Premier criti
cized America's unjustified conduct and
said that everything might be dis
cussed except the war. because the
-cases of the generals were still sub
judice.
Count d'Almenas resumed his attack
upon the generals and complained that
"five months had elapsed and not a
single general been shot."
This gave rise to another tumult and
Count d'Almenas was called to order.
Again he asked why the generals who
capitulated had not been executed. It
is quite true, he declared that the army
is an army of lions led by asses. Cap
tain General Blanco? administration in
Cuba was deplorable, he said, but he
was not responsible for the surrender
of Santiago.
MORE POWERFUL
THAN DYNAMITE
New Explosive Powder i
Invented.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA, Feb. 20.â Dr. H. P. Tuttle
of this city is the Inventor of a new
explosive powder, which he believes
will go far toward revolutionizing na
val warfare. It is more powerful than j
nitroglycerin or dynamite, and yet
may be handled with perfect safety.
Dr. Tuttle returned yesterday from a
two months' trip to Washington City, j
where he has been in conference with j
the army ordnance board regarding his I
new powder. What results were at
tained there he will not say, because
the ordnance board, he says, has re
quested him to keep silent.
Before laying the matter before the
United States ordnance board, Tuttle I
entertained Admiral Pellisere of , the
British squadron at Esquimalt with an
exhibition of his powder, and the mat
ter was referred- to the British Admir
alty for discussion and investigation.
At Esquimalt he first exploded some
of his powder in the open, demonstrat
ing its terrible destructive force. Then
he undertook to show that it was safe
to handle. The party returned to the
machine shops, where Doctor Tuttle
made the somewhat startling state
ment that his powder could be pounded
on an anvil with a sledge hammer <t
thrust through with a red-hot iron and
yet would not explode. Experiments
proved these assertions to be true.
Striking a pound of it with a sledge
hammer had no more effect than
pounding so much sand.
A cannon on board the flagship was
next loaded with <>ne of Tuttle's shells,
which was thrown against the sitie of
a rocky cliff a quarter of a mile away.
It mashed to pieces on the rocks and
tore up such a quantity of dust and dirt
that the captain of the flagship avowed
that it exploded.
"No, it did not,"' maintained Dr. Tut
tle; "that was only the shock of tho
impact. I will show you the differ
ence."
So, loading another shell with a pri
mer of ordinary powder, it was let go
against the rock. This time there was
no chance for an argument. A roar
louder than that of the cannon came
back, while for hundreds of feet the
air was full of dust and flir* and
chunks of rock, some of them a ton in
weight, flew in every direction.
This fully established Tuttle's claim
that his powder surpassed dynamite in
explosive force. Tuttle's cannon, which
was made out of a car axle, was then
fir^d, using a shell about an inch in
diameter. It was aimed at a half-inch
steel plate 100 yards away. A sharp
hole was cut nearly through the plate,
to the surprise of the Entcliph officers.
Tuttle began experimenting witÂ£ ex
plosives two yeara ago, being requested
by friends to produce an explosive that
. could be us^d successfully in the Klon
dike. He says rec- -" his powder:
"It means the end of steel-clads.
With it shells can be sent through the
sides of warships as eaailv as solid shot
is now sent through, and, explodin" in
side the vessel, w<>ulJ tear it to atoms."

there was renewed disorder.
Then General Primo de Rivera arose
and denounced Count d'Almenas as a
"contemptible calumniator." General
Blanco l'nllowed, defending the gener
als and accepting full responsibility for
events in Cuba during his command in
the island. General Barges also de
nounced. Count cl'Almenas as a calumni
ator.
After a promise on the part of
Count d'Almenas to produce proofs of
his assertions to-morrow the Senate ad
journed.
In the Chamber the resolution of con
dolence of France over the death of
President Faure was unanimously
adopted.
Senor Silvela moved a vote signed by
the Conservative Deputies censuring
this Government for its indifference to
the country's troubles.
Senor Annix, in seconding: the motion,
denounced the Ministry for accepting
the war through fear of the Carlists.
and declared that the country was now
suffering the consequences of the Gov
ernment's pusillanimity. He proceeded
to detail the lack of preparations and
of war materials, and charged the Gov
ernment with responsibility for the sur
render of Santiago, "which they or
dered, although the garrison there num
bered 23,000 and there was sufficient
provisions in the place for three
months."
This declaration created a sensation.
In proof that the Government was re- j
sponsible for the surrender of Cuba
Senor Annix read telegrams from Senor
Sagasta and Lieutenant General Correa
to General Blanco ordering the sur
render of Cuba as a means of saving
Porto Rico and the Philippines and pre
serving order in the peninsula. He also
read General Blanco's telegram in re
ply, opposing the surrender, but agree
ing to obey the Government's order.
Senor Annix added that President i
McKinley had telegraphed to General
Shafter that the surrender of Santiago
had been arranged with the Madrid
Government, and that, therefore, he
must make a sort of sham attack.
Captain Aunon, Minister of Marine, !
interposed at this point, saying he be
lieved this story to be incorrect; where- i
upon Senor Annix repeated the state- j
ment that Premier Sagasta had ordered
the surrender of Cuba in order to save
the monarchy.
GOLD FOUND ON
MOUNTAIN TOPS
Rich Bench Claims Near
Dawson.
BY HAL HOFFMAN.
Special Correspondence of The Call.
JUNEAU, Alaska, Feb. 13 (via Seat
tle, Feb. 16). â "Jack" Hanley, now here,
fourteen and a half days from Dawson,
Is much chagrined at not having beaten
ihe record but. He is one of the lucky
young men who have all the gold they
can dig out of the ground in the next
two or three years. It has been some
time since a gold story has come out
of Dawson. Hanley says some of the
bench claims, almost literally on top of
the mountains, are proving as rich as
many in the bottoms of the creeks, and
this fact almost takes the breath out
of the bodies of old miners, it is so un
precedented. One of these top-moun
tain claim owners, Martin Walstad, is
now here and sick. Neither of these
young men know how much he is
worth. Both knocked around Juneau
several years, taking what work they
could get in winter to buy grub to pros
pect on in summer, not knowing the
store of gold ahead of them.
Hanley says the total output of the
Klondike mines next clean-up in the
spring will exceed that of last year by
several millions. One reason for this
is that a good deal of ground was not
worked last year on account of the
royalty. Hope of its being reduced
soon has been abandoned and succeed
ed by the conclusion that life is too
short and there is too much of a good
time ahead to wait longer on the possi
bility of saving a few thousand dollars.
The situation at Dawson is about the
same. The town is comparatively
quiet.
"They are not coming in bunches as
they were last winter." is the way Han
ley puts it. "Still Dawson is all right
and will be a good camp for some
years, but I do not think it ever again
will be the town it was last winter and
summer."
This supports the statement made to
me by another Klondiker two weeks
ago, that Dawson has seen her best
days.
Klondiker Hanley said experienced
miners would never prospect in Cana
dian territory again, which accounts
for so many going to Eagle City and
elsewhere down the Yukon. This is
on account of the royalty, the licensee
required, the size of claims (250 feet),
with ten claims below Discovery re
served by the crown, as well as every
alternate ten claims.
Many men are still out of work at
Dawson, but they have plenty of food.
Hundreds are on the trail coming out.
The trail is in good shape and travel
ing comparatively easy.
Some new towns are springing up
on the river. At the present rate of
development there will be towns in a
year or two not very far apart all the
way to Dawson. Quite a settlement is
springing up at the mouth or the Ste
wart River and another at Thistle
Creek. Substantial buildings are be
ing erected. Hanley says he talked
with several acquaintances on the way
out who had claims on Thistle Creek
and all expected good pay in the spring.
Canadian mall has gone down the
river quite regularly during the winter,
but the American mail has been al
lowed to stack up and, figuratively,
grow moldy, chiefly at Tagish Lake,
since the middle of October last. It is
welcome news to thousands in the
States that the American mail is now
beginning to move. Hanley met at Le
barge dog teams carrying about a ton
of it and these teams will get through
to Dawson all right, if they are not al
ready at Dawson by the time this shall
have been published. Information 1 is
meager as to how regularly the mail
will keep moving. There i 3 need for the
postal department at Washington to
i get a "move on" In making conjunction
1 with the Canadian postal authorities

WASHINGTON, Feb. 20.â The Miles

mittee in the Lemon building, with ac

the press associations and the local pa

dent and the general commanding the
army occupied a large part of the fore
noon, and immediately thereafter Gen
eral Nelson A. Miles appeared as the
first witness. He and three other army
officers summoned as witnesses were
examined and disposed of before the
court closed its session at 3 o'clock.
General Miles' testimony, of course, was
the feature of the day's proceedings.
Colonel Frank Micheler, his chief of
staff, and a gentleman in citizen's
clothes, who took a seat by the fire
place and gravely watched the course
of proceedings, retiring with the gen
eral as silently as he had come, ac
companied the general to the court
room.
General Miles appeared in fatigue
uniform, rather striking in its contrast
with the display of scarfs, bullion and
buttons on the full dress uniforms of
the court. He submitted to the ques
tioning of Colonel Davis, recorder of
the court, with equanimity, except for
a flash of mingled indignation and
irony when he informed the recorder
that he was not compelled to "report"
to the commissary general of the army, j
This was in reply to a question as to
whether or not he had reported to the
Commissary Department the fact that
complaints had been made to him as !
to the quality of meat being issued to
the troops. An instant later General
Miles resumed his cool and carefully j
considered replies to the inquiries of <
the court.

taken out. It appeared to have been
boiled to pieces."

GREAT RUSH IS
NOT JUSTIFIED
Captain Ray's Report on
the Klondike.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Feb. 20.â Secretary
Alger has transmitted to Congress a
â¢complete report on the relief work of
the War Department in the Klondike
country undertaken under act of Con
gress as a result of reports that ex
treme distress among the miners fol
lowed the influx to the gold country
' during 1597-9 S. It embraces the detailed
-reports of Captain P. H. Ray and Lieu
: tenant W. P. Richardson Aside from
details of the relief work Captain Ray a
Xt report, dated May last, contains |
the following interesting statement.
I deem it of the greatest toportance iter
the development of the countn that
roads should be opened so as to enable
the people to enter the country not onl>
supplies. Under existing conditions per
eons can enter the country only by the
one big highwav-fhe Yukon Rlver-and
by that they cannot hope to be landed at
â  a *Â¥he POi gold- bearing Uly dlstrlcts are from
The gold-bearing districts are from
fifty to one hundred and fifty miles back
from the main stream. There are not any
summer trails except foot trails, and pro
â  Sand mining tools can only be
transported on the backs of men. I do
: rot find anything either in Alaska or in
. the Northwest Territory to justify the
ereat rush of people to that country,
or the enormous investment now being
made in transportation, trading and min
ing companies. In Northwest Territory
no discoveries of extraordinary richness
have been made .since that of the Klon
dike, and the claims are almost entirely
confined to Bonanza. El Dorado, Domin
ion, Hunker and Sulphur, with a few
claims on Bear Creek. In this district all
â¢rich claims are well known and held at
very high prices, and while the whole
'" country has been staked it has been done
. for speculative purposes, and no work
. Is' being done except such as is necessary
to hold a title until they can be sold to
the unwary newcomer or' disposed of in
the States for corporation schemes. Cap
â¢ tain Constantine of the Canadian North
western police is my authority for de
' nouncing the movement as a fraud.
â¢ In the absence, of any other Industry,
. except cutting wood for the river boats,
I do not see anything In the future for
â¢ over 90 per cent of the people now flock
.'.lng to that country but disappointment
and suffering. Even those who obtain
â¢'.employment at $150 per hour find that
"after deducting the cost of food, pack
â¢ Ing', candles, etc., they do not net to ex
â¢ ceed $2 per day, and they tell mc It is
-.barely enough to tide them over the idle
.-: season.
â¢'.."Up to date no paying mineral leads of
either gold or silver have been discovered
â¢- In North Alaska, so far as known. lam
cow fully satisfied that In the near future
"-.Alaska will be the source or great wealth,
...but the development will necessarily be
Slow, owing to the climatic conditions. I
â¢recommend the early Introduction of
"horses, mules and cattle and extra In
ducements should be held out for the de
â¢ velopment of agriculture in the valley of
â  the Lower Yukon especially. Wild hay
â¢ can be obtained there in great abund
.ance and oats, barley and spring wheat
can be successfully cultivated, as well as
potatoes, turnips and all trie more hardy
garden vegetables, all of which would be
. .required for many years to meet the local
' -demand.
. .But few of the people now entering the
if northern part of the territory will ever
â¢ -become a factor In its permanent de
â¢' velopment. They must pass away before
'â¢the time when the wealth of the country
'â¢ will become known and developed. To
promote this I recommend that several
â¢ well-equipped parties be put In the field
â¢ and a thorough geographical and geo
logical exploration of the country be
made so that men of ordinary means will
be able to engage in the work of pros
â¢ pecting and mining. At present it re
quires considerable capital to first explore
' the country for a practical route to trans
port supplies before any work can be done
in prospecting.
â¢ The ruling rate of Interest at Dawson is
â¢ from 10 to 12 per cent per month, which
is the best exemplification I can give of
â " the speculating condition of the finances
of that country.

CHILDREN VISIT
THE CITRUS FAIR
Hundreds of Youngsters
in the Pavilion.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CLOVERDALE, Feb. 2Â©.âClover
dales midwinter festival continues
merrily, and this day was the merriest
since the opening. Nearly 400 school
children as guests of the Citrus Fair
Association, from the stately, self-im
portant lads and lassies of the high
j school to the little tets just opening
their eyes to life's possibilities, roamed
through the tropical environments of
the pavilion, receiving an object lesson
in the possibilities of the Golden West.
The children enjoyed the day, flitting i
frum one exhibit to another In open- !
mouthed amazement at the pretty
sights. The candy and icecream, and !
especially the fish pond booths, did a
large business.
The musical programme consisted
mostly of national airs and natriotic
selections, instilling into the Juvenile
mind love of flag- and country;
The weather continues beautiful, the
roads are fine and many visitors drove
to town from points miles distant.
There was a concert to-night and the
pavilion was crowded. The railroad,
company is pressing every available
car into service for Sonoma day, when
an enormous crowd will visit Clover
dale. I

SPIRITED REPLY TO
LORD BERESFORD

at Talien Wan.
NEW YORK, Feb. 20.â The Washing
ton correspondent of the Herald tele
graphs: I obtained to-day from Count
Cassini, Russian Embassador to the
United States, an interview in answer
to the statements made by Lord Charles
Beresford relative to the establishment
of an open door in China.
Count Cassini was transferred from
Peking to Washington after five and a
half years of service at the Chinese
capital and his views are highly re
garded here. Before talking with me
respecting Lord Beresford's statements
the Embassador said of the report pub
lished this morning that in a conflict
between Russian and Chinese troops at
Talien Wan 300 of the latter were
killed: "I do not believe the report. If
there had been any foundation for it I
would have been immediately advised
of the facts by my Government. The
Chinese have no troops at Talien V, an
and a conflict could not therefore have
occurred." Â

declarations in favor of the mainte

RKDONDOâ SaiIed Feb 20â Stmr Grace Dol
lai. for San Francisco.
Beresford does not say at present there
exists a closed door, but says that it
is possible that in future there may be
a closed door. As a matter of fact
China holds in her hands authority to
declare whether there shall be an open
or a closed door.
"So far as Russia is concerned it is
to her interest to have free trade in the
Far East. It is necessary in view of the
construction of the trans-Siberian rail
! road that there should be an extension
t of commercial relations in order that
the road can be operated on a paying
basis.
"The policy of Russia in the extreme
Orient has always favored the introduc
tion of foreign commerce on an equal
footing. Vladivostok is maintained as
a free port. Talien Wan, which ife within
the Russian sphere of influence in
China, is to-day a free port.
"Lord Charles Beresford states that
Russia wants to take possession of the
north of China and leave the south of
that country to France. Now he pro
poses that England, the United States,
Germany and Japan shall take charge
of affairs in China, England to have
control of the army, customs, etc., a
very natural desire from his point of
view.
"Why does he make exceptions of
Russia and France? He does so on the
ground that they are not commercial
peoples. This is a mistake. France is
a commercial nation and Russia will be.
The Siberian road is a commercial idea.
He says it is built only for strategic rea
sons. T-his is another mistake."
NEALL'S RAPID
CAREER AT RENO
Lieutenant Left Many
Creditors.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
RENO, New, Feb. 20.â Lieutenant
"Jack" M. Neall. whose sudden disap
pearance from the Presidio has created
such a furor in San Francisco, haa a
little history in Reno which by way of
coincidence sheds some light upon" the
episodes before the public-
Lieutenant Neall lived in Reno for
four years, ending 1894, and in the ca
pacity of military Instructor and all
around professor at the Nevada Uni
versity won the esteem and good will
of nearly all the best people here. His
affable, genial ways gained the devotion
of the students who attended his
classes. But his Nemesis overtook him
and much of this popularity was lost
when the time came for his departure
hence and he was called upon to settle
accounts. It then developed that his
mania for gambling and for a fast life
generally had led - him into debt and
when he left here bankers, merchants,
saloon-keepers and people of many
classes were his creditors in sums vary
ing from $1 to $100 or more, and aggre
gating over U2OO, The Washoe County
Bank was about the only one of these
claimants which held any security bet
ter than Neall's fickle word.
[ The bank got its money, but C. Nova
covich. the grocer; W. O. H. Martin the
general merchant; H. J. Theys, the
saloon-keeper, and many others had ac
counts against Neall that could not be
collected. Efforts were made through
the War Department and through in
j slstent collecting agencies, but finally
! the greater portion of these claims were
charged to profit and loss.
Neall's reputation as an efficient
teacher of military tactics as well as
ether branches In which he had. classes
was first class, and aside from his un
fortunate sporting proclivities he would
have ranked here among the best.

FOR CONSTRUCTION OF
THE PACIFIC CABLE
TV ABHINGTON, Feb. 20.-Senator Frye !
to-day reported from the Committee on
Foreign Relations the amendment to the !
Bundr>- civil bill for the construction of
a submarine cable in the Paolflc Oct-an
as amended -by the committee The
amendments are numerous and radi-al
The moÃt important of them increases the
subsidy to be paid every year for twenty
y l a . r l t( l * m m and extends the time in
which the contracting company is to be
given to complete the line to the Philin
pine Islands to the Ist of January 1902
Other amendments increase the 'forfeit
of the contracting company trom $200 000 :
to $400,000; provide that the subsidy
money shall only be used in maintenance
and repairs and not on paying dividends*
fix the rates on Government messages at
one-third the rates on private messages
retaining the provision that they shall be
carried free during the twenty years; fix
a rate of 25 cents per word on private
messasrpp to Hawaii and $1 per word on
such messages to the Philippines, press
rates being one-fourth of these amounts;
give the Postmaster General the right to
revise these rates every ten years, and
authorize the President to request the
co-operation of Japan in securing the es
tablishment of the proposed line.
