LUMBER AND THE
ALASKA BOUNDARY
Stumbling Blocks of the
Joint Commission.
AN ADJOURNMENT TAKEN
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Feb. 20.â The Ameri
can-Canadian joint high commission,
after a session to-day, adjourned to
meet at (Juebec, August 2, unless the
chairmen of the respective commissions
agree upon another date. There were
several reasons which prompted the ac
tion taken to-day.
From a statement made public with
the sanction of the two commissions it
appears there were serious differences
of opinion between them regarding the
important matter of the delimitation of
the Alaskan boundary, the Canadians
in this connection being anxious to ob
tain a seaport in Lynn canal so that its
shippers would not be subjected to the
petty annoyances which they asserted
they experienced at the hands of United
States officials. Then the time for the
meeting cf the Canadian Parliament, at
which the presence of members of the
commission from that country is de
sired, is overdue, and the latter are
anxious to return as soon as possible.
It also became apparent that there was
little likelihood of ratification by the
United States Senate at the present ses
sion of any treaty which might be
framed, which also probably figured in
the decision to adjourn.
A dozen important questions were re
ferred to the commission for its con
sideration and the commissioners state
that substantial progress In the settle
ment and adjustment of many of the
questions has been made. Probably
those which have given the most seri
ous trouble and proved a stumbling
block to a final agreement on all have
been the two relating to lumber and the
Alaskan boundary question, on both of
which the Canadians have demanded
concessions which the Americans felt
it would be prejudicial to the best in
terests of the United States to grant.
It was felt also that an adjournment
for a few months might result in a bet
ter prospect for harmony and a dispo
sition on the part of the Canadians to
agree to propositions made to them by
the commissioners on this side of the
boundary line.
Senator Bairbanks, chairman of the
American commission, has repeatedly
stated that there was no basis for the
reports of a rupture in the relations of
the two bodies and to-night supple
mented this by saying that they were
on the best of terms. On behalf of their
respective commissions Senator Fair
banks, the chairman of the American
board, and Sir Wilfrid Laurier, acting
chairman in the absence of Lord Her
schell, who is ill, of the Canadian board,
made the following public statement
respecting the action taken to-day:
The commission adjourned to meet at
Quebec August 2 unless the chairmen of
the respective committees shall agree
upon another date. The commission has
made very substantial progress in the set
tlement and adjustment of many of the
?;utstions upon which it has been earnest
y engaged. But it has been unable to
agree upon the settlement of the Alaskan
boundary. This problem is complicated
and difficult, but the commissions, acting
in the utmost friendliness and cordiality,
have been unable to agree upon a satis
factory adjustment. The difficulties, apart
from the immediate delimitation of this
boundary by the commission itself, arise
from the conditions under which it might
be referred to arbitration. The British
commissioners desired that the whole
question should be referred on terms sim
ilar to those provided in the reference of
of the Venezuela boundary line and which
by providing sin umpire would insure cer
tainty and finality. The United States
commissioners, on the other hand,
thought the local conditions in Alaska so
different that some modifications of the
Venezuelan boundary reference should be
introduced. They thought the reference
should be made to the six eminent jur
ists, three chosen by each of the high
contracting parties, without providing- for
an umpire, they believing that flnality
would be secured by a majority vote of
the jurists so chosen. They did not see
any present prospect of agreeing to a
European umpire to be selected in the
manner proposed by the British commis
sioners, while the British commissioners
were unwilling to agree to the selection
of an American umpire in the manner
suggested by the United States commis
sioners. The United States commissioners
further contended that special stipula
tions should be made in any reference
to arbitration that the existing /settle
ments on the tidewaters of the coast
should in any result continue to belong to
the United States. To this contention the
British commissioners refused to agree.
It was therefore deemed advisable to ad
journ to a convenient date in order to
enable the respective governments to fur
ther consider the subject with respect to
which no conclusion has yet been re
ported.

WOMAN BATTLES
WITH THREE MEN
CL.OVERDALE, Feb. 20.â A criminal case was commenced in the
Justice Court here to-day which is arousing considerable interest.
About two years ago Charles Norman of San Francisco patented 160
acres of Government land six miles west of Cloverdale. He built a
cabin and remained there a few months, but not long enough, it is
claimed, to entitle him to the land according to the homestead laws.
C H. Hill and wife of San Francisco settled upon this abandoned
homestead a few months ago and were going though the regular pro
cess of law to claim it as their own when Norman appeared last Satur
day to order them off. He was accompanied by George J. Mathews, an
extensive land owner in the vicinity and James Clements
* Mr Hill not being at home, his wife met them at the door with a
shotgun and a positive refusal to leave the place. .Mathews and Nor
man tried to take the gun from her. She fought valiantly and in the
Â«triurele three of her ribs were broken, her hands terribly lacerated and
she was internally injured. Physicians are doubtful as to her recovery.
Warrants for the arrest of the three men were sworn out yester
day but for want of evidence on both sides the preliminary trial was
continued to-day by Judge Smith to March 6, and the defendants were
held under $5000 bonds each.
Fx-Dlstrlc -Utorney Emmett Sewall of Santa Rosa and G. W . Hoyle
of Cloverdale are the attorneys for the defendants, District Attorney
O. O. Webber conducting the prosecution.

ANOTHER VICTIM OF THE TRACK-
â 
lIEUTENANT NEALL of the Fourth Cavalry
is now an exile, or. dead by his own hand, his I
. â * body rests somewhere beyond the reach of
searchers.
:" â¢-" This lieutenant was an able and popular officer.
His men had for him a feeling little less than affection.
/â¢â .They gladly obeyed his orders. They would have j
. â¢ followed him to the field of battle and felt that they I
;;had a leader. Among gentlemen in civil life, as in
;. .military, he found a ready welcome. He was edu- \
:..."Cated, genial, magnetic, and had a way of winning j
. friends. Yet now, so be that he is dead or wander
â lo.ing, there rests upon his name and honor the brand
.". (if "thief and embezzler. Trust has been betrayed,
â¢ cast aside.
It is worth while to inquire how ail this has hap
â¢ pened, and the explanation is easy. Lieutenant Neall
was merely another victim of the insatiate races j
. " Which every day furnish trap and bait, luring the j
:.. gullible to destruction. He thought he could win.
- He thought that by shrewd betting he could lift him
self from the pit into which extravagant habits had i
â¢ led him, and the result the inevitable result â the i
'. ' result which has become an old story â was the fate
â  awaiting him.
V. Add the name of Xeall to the long list. Add it i
to that of Widber, of Figel, of Norton, the suicide, j
1 and Welborn, the defaulter. There are countless more |
.- v.ho more quietly have achieved the loss of place and j
â¢ reputation and opportunity through these same
< races â a continual temptation, a bootless hope.
â¢ The downfall of Neall is a sad instance of that
.:. which a continual racing season is accomplishing j
1 â¢ for San Francisco. Does the public know that women
walk to the track so as to save their precious nickels
â¢to wager on some race which has. been decided before
. the start of the horses? Does it know that clerks and
bookkeepers clutch their salaries greedily so as to be
" able to give all to the cormorants of the track? Such
. are the simple, sorry facts.
, San Francisco pays daily thousands of dollars for
" the. support of an iniquitous institution which fattens j
: a lot of greedy and conscienceless speculators, here |
â  and there helps a '"plunger" to an unearned fortune, ;
. sustains a lot of vagrants and touts, the while doing
â¢'.no good to any legitimate industry, adding nothing
'.to "the sum of wealth, teaching nothing as to the art \
â¢ â  of breeding. And San Francisco is paying too high .
â¢a figure for disgrace, crime, pauperism. It is time
for. the racetracks to be limited in their capacity to
.rob. If permitted to operate a few days each year
they might do no harm. As institutions conducted
with no time limit, they have fearful power to at-
t tract and destroy. Neall is only a sample. There are
enough others to make the heart sick.

THE INTERESTS OF PARTY-
THE people who are attempting to force Dan
Burns into the Senate are suffering an acute
attack of party loyalty. The Record-Union
has discovered that the people demand a Republican
Senator and will punish the party if one is not de
livered. As this is the voice of the Southern Pacific
Railroad, we desire to answer it. that there would
have been a Republican Senator early in the session
if the Southern Pacific Railroad had kept faith with
the people and made good the word of the Republican
part}- that that corporation was out of politics, (/rant
ing the great necessity for the election of a Republican
Senator, the seat to be filled in the Senate belo
to the party and the people and not to the Southern
Pacific Railroad. No friend of that company, no
stockholder in it, no decent man authorized to S]
for it, can give any reason or make any apology tor
its selection of Dan Burns and its attempt to elect
him Senator. No such thing was dreamed oi before
the election. Had it been hinted at the Republican
party would have lost the State, li he is elected now
no power on earth can save the State to the party in
iooo. Every man of sense connected with the rail
road knows this. To elect Burns means sure defeat
Ihe Record-Union shivers with fear of what may
nay come if no Senator is elected. We agree with it
that one should be chosen. But an Oregon Repub
lican Legislature refused to eleel an objectionable
man, and rather than do a disgraceful thing did noth
ing. The people did not punish the party for it.
On the contrary, they doubled its majority at the ,
next election and enabled it to elect a decent man j
and statesman to the Federal Senate.
Things are at a bad stage if the people are ready to
kill a party for being decent, but will give it further
confidence only on condition that it will be dirty.
We repeat, let the Southern Pacific Company draw
out of this fight, let the Legislature alone and attend
t i its railroad business, and there can be a Republican
Senator in forty-eight hours.
We and the whole Republican press insisted last
fall that the railroad was out of Â£>olitics. So did Gov
ernor Gage and every candidate and speaker of the
Republican party. We don't know how others feel
about it, but we don't like the railroad to make as a!!
liars.
THE STANFORD UNIVERSITY.
THE Stanford University, which is magnificently
endowed for higher education, although not a
State institution, in any legal sen^e, must always
| be an object of pride and of solicitude to the people
of the State, and we appreciate and admire the en
thusiasm of men who, for its benefit, are willing to
I turn a section of a State constitution into a piece of
special legislation. Still we are compelled to adhere
, to the opinion we expressed some weeks ago against
I the exemption of the property of the university from
' taxation. The question of State aid to institutions of
i learning and of tlje relief of property used for that
purpose from the burden of taxation was much dis
cussed and considered in the Constitutional Conven
tion of 1879. The conclusions of that body were ex
pressed in the constitution adopted in that year as it
now exists, and are a part of the system then devised
for the avowed object of equalizing the pressure of
taxation. By article IV, section 22, all appropriations
for institutions not under the exclusive management
j and control of the State, except those caring for or
phans, half-orphans, abandoned children and indigent
persons, are expressly prohibited. By article X, section
â¢ 9, it will be seen that even the University of California
: is not entirely a State institution, but that it is declared
to be a ''public trust," subject partly to legislative
control. By section 12 of article XI the Legislature is
debarred from doing more than vesting in counties,
1 towns and cities by general laws the power of assess
irg and collecting taxes for local use. Article XIII,
section 1. enacts that "all property in the State, not ex
! empt under the laws of the United States, shall be
taxed," and the sixth section of the same article pro
vides that "the power of taxation shall never be sur
i rendered or suspended by any grant or contract to
i which the State shall be a party."
These quotations suggest at least the derangements
that would inevitably flow from the exemption of the
Stanford University, and of the colossal fortune it
i represents, from all compensation for the protection
it receives under the law. The property from which
the income of the university is derived is situated in
different counties and is a competing factor in some of
the chief industries of the State. The escape of that
property from taxation would deprive those counties
I of needed income and would increase the burden of
I other property owners in particular localities, so that
gross inequality would be the consequences. Even
I though the Stanford University were technically a
! Siate institution, this would be unjust Any advau
i tage it might derive over other owners of property
I should be held at least to an equitable basis, and
i should be charged proportionately to the whole num
: her within the State. Nor should competitors in
I business be exposed to the inequality of paying their
taxes while the Stanford University is set free.
We are reluctantly opposed, therefore, to the pro
posed amendment to relieve the Stanford University
from taxation. If any such exemption could be tole
-1 rated, it ought to apply to all similar institutions, and
! be formulated in a general proposition that in its
! application would leave to the Legislature the exer
! cise of a reasonable discretion. In recent years State
1 constitutions, instead of supplying brief and compre
; hensive limitations and directions, have been lavishly
: employed for special legislation. This is a radical
[ objection to the amendment under discussion, but so
far as the investment of the Stanford University with
power to receive donations from any source is con-
I ctrned, upon the showing made we see no escape from
.; the conclusion that the amendment should be pro-
I posed and adopted.
THE INITIATIVE fIND THE REFER
ENDUM.
WHILE the Populists and their allies are talk
ing of making a campaign of education in the
State in favor of the incorporation into our
political system of the initiative and the referendum,
it appears the operation of our constitution has vir
tually brought the system into vogue already.
The constitution removes from the Legislature and
embodies in the organic law so many matters requir
ing revision from time to time that the submission
tc the people of proposals for amending the constitu
tion has become a common practice. At every State
election there has been for a number of years past
several such amendments to be voted upon. This
practice is in the fullest sense of the word the use of
the referendum, and the range of questions which it
covers is about as broad as any one except an ex
tremist can desire.
The constitution does not provide for the initiative,
but the evolution of politics has of itself brought
that about. The submission of constitutional amend
ments has become so common that there is now
hardly any serious objection to the practice. Even
persons who are opposed to the particular measure
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, FEBRUARY 21, 1899.
proposed are generally in favor of submitting it and
giving the people an opportunity to vote upon it.
As a result of this condition of public sentimen.t the
people have a large power of initiative. When any
considerable number of influential persons desire a
constitutional amendment submitted to .the popular
vote, they can obtain the concession without much
difficulty. It is not incumbent upon them to show
that any particular proportion of the voters desire to
have the issue submitted. It is simply necessary to
demonstrate by petition or otherwise that a strong
body of citizens favor the proposition in order to
get the Legislature to concede it.
In most States of the Union the popular vote on
constitutional amendments does not amount to an
effective referendum, because comparatively few sub
jects are covered by the organic law. In California,
however, the State constitution is more like a code of
laws than a true constitution, and accordingly there is
in it a wide variety of issues to be submitted to the
popular vote. The practice has now become so well
settled it will hardly be broken up, and although pro
posals to amend the constitution are nearly always
beaten at the polls, we shall unquestionably have such
questions submitted in greater or less numbers at
every election so long as the present constitution
.stands.
THE SENATORIAL SCANDALS.
IT IS doubtful whether any proposition in politics
was ever more thoroughly settled than the very
late one o! electing United States Senators by pop
ular vote. There is no question that if the Federal
Senate is to continue to enjoy the respect of
the country and its legislation to receive the acquies
cence <>i the masses a change will have to be effected
in the mode of electing its members.
It is really getting so that a Senator who reaches
his station without scandal becomes a veritable curi
ositj among statesmen. Outside the old States, where
some of the traditions of the fathers still con
tinue to be the rule of action, no United States Sena
tors are elected nowadays without the assistance of
money, political corruption and debauchery.
Things have been a little worse this year than ever
before. In three Western Legislatures not only have
there been open charges of bribery, but investigating
committees have actually accumulated evidence to
sustain them. Only the other day a candidate before
the Utah Legislature was accused of offering $1500
a vote, and less than a month ago a member of the
Montana Legislature walked to the Speaker's desk
and handed over a roll of greenbacks, which had been
given him by another candidate. In the California
Legislature two members have been convicted of ac
cepting the money of a Senatorial candidate prior to
the election, and in both cases the moral turpitude
.of the act has not seemed to shock anybody.
It is impossible that such things as these should
continue and the principal legislative body of the
country survive. Either the power to elect Senators
must be taken from the Legislatures, which have
proved entirely unworthy of the trust, or a radical
change is bound to take place in the character of the
Federal Senate. It is incredible that men who reach
positions in that body by the use of money can be
come honest or trustworthy afterward. No stream
ever rose above its source. A debauched and cor
rupted Legislature never produced a Senator capable
of representing an honest and moral people.
The question is, how to bring this reform about.
Petitions and mass meetings have been suggested,
and perhaps in their way they might do some good.
Votes of the people which, so far as taken, indicate
an overwhelming public sentiment in favor of a
change, might also tend to produce the reform. But
something stronger than all this is necessary. If the
people knew the danger which beset them of having
ultimately a Senate composed entirely of corruption
ists and boodlers, they would arise en masse and wipe
the reproach off the face of the country. Perhaps we
shall be forced to await the formation of a public
opinion that will manifest itself in this way. In the
meantime, . however, all good citizens should work
uniformly for the reform. The preservation of their
liberties demand that they should pull in this direc
tion all together.
PROSPECTS OF AN EXTRA SESSION.
SUCH conflicting reports come from Washington
concerning the outlook for an extra session of
Congress it would seem the issue is to be left
for time and chance to determine. Statements from
men high in authority show a wide disagreement.
Some are sanguine we shall have an extra session,
some are equally sanguine we shall not, while others
frankly admit they do not know.
Not long ago Secretary Alger was quoted as saying
if the army reorganization bill were not passed there
would surely be an extra session. Secretary Gage also
is reported as sharing that opinion. In a recent inter
view he is stated to have said: "Will there be an
extra session? That depends on the fate of the army
bill. If Congress does not pass that bill at this session
there will certainly be an extra session."
On the other hand, Senator Hanna is reported to
have stated to an interviewer in New York: "An ex
tra session is not at all probable. Our Democratic
friends would like to force us into one, so as to help
them 1 out of their free silver muddle, but they are
going to be disappointed on that score." Proceeding
further with the question and referring to the demand
for an extra session to deal with the issue of monetary
reform, the Senator is quoted as saying:
"Some of our financial men here in New York City
came over to Washington not long ago, urging an
extra session of Congress to pass monetary legislation.
When they realized what such a session would mean,
however, and heard the opinion of the country outside
of New York, they became entirely satisfied to trust
to the judgment of the administration. As I under
stand the present position, it is that nothing but an
emergency far beyond anything now in sight will
justify the President in calling Congress together,
possibly for the whole summer, and reopening debate
on all manner of questions."
This statement with respect to financial legislation
is not at all in accord with the views attributed to
Secretary Gage, who is reported to have said on the
occasion of his recent visit to Chicago:
"Financial reform is neither dead nor asleep. The
extraordinary prosperity of the Government finances
and of the business of the country has combined with
the glut of important business in Congress to delay it.
But the delay only adds to the sense of its importance,
and makes it clearer every day that legislation is
needed."
From the confusion of these reports the only con
clusion that can be drawn is that the administration is
keeping its own council, and is awaiting develop
ments. Despite the statements attributed to Senator
Hanna the chances are we shall have an extra session.
Some of the measures before Congress are too im
portant to be kept waiting, and it is not likely they can
be dealt with at this session. The people wish the
Nicaragua Canal bill, the Hawaiian Government bill,
the shipping bill and a currency reform bill enacted
into law; and their postponement for another year
would be a grass blunder.
TOKEN OF GRATITUDE
FROM BOYS AT SEA
Clock Given The Call by the Monterey's Crew.
IF a thing of beauty is a joy forever.
The Call office will never know sor
row. It has received from the crew
of the Monterey, most graciously and
unexpectedly bestowed, a gift which
while in itself valuable, the finest work
of the skilled craftsman, gains an addi
tional vaiue from the method of its be-
Btowal. The brief letter accompanying
it explains fully the motive, but it mod
estly avoids any reference to the quality
(ri the present.
Probably there is not in America an
other such clock, perhaps not another in
the world, like that brought by the Va
lencia. It is in the form of a warship,
very suggestive. of the Monterey, only the
hull is proportionately higher. The hull
duwn to the water line is of blued steel,
and below of brass polished to mirror
luster. Fore and aft are turrets, each
mounting two guns. The turrets are of
steel, capped and embellished with shin
ing brass. The forward one has upon one
side a clock, and the after one a barom
eter. Both turrets revolve constantly.
Upon the two smokestacks, finished also
in steel and brass, are thermometers,
one Fahrenheit and the other centigrade.
Chains of brass encircle the capstan and
depend on either side. The capstan is
surmounted by a compass scientifically
correct. The anchor chains and the an
chors are of steel or nickel, as are the
guns protruding warlike from the sides
of the hull. The steering gear is com
plete and the propeller whirls unceasing
ly. The rudder is of brass, with steel
straps, and all over the miniature vessel
every bolt is in place, and every rivet.
This tiny Monterey is equipped with a
ram which dn a real vessel would carry
terror to the enemy. The guard rail la
of silver ropes upheld by brass posts.
Not a detail has been omitted. At the
prow a metal Union Jack makes a pre
tense of waving, and at the stern the
stars and stripes do the same. The
whole artistic mechanism is mounted on
a pedestal of green onyx set off with gold,
and on one side is an ornate silver plate
bearing the inscription:
"Presented to the San Francisco Call by
the crew of the l r . S. S. Monterey, Ma
nila, P. 1., January, 1899."
One strange feature of the ship is that
there is nowhere on it a mark whereby
can be learned what cunning hands
wrought it. But the work would be a
credit to the genius of any nationality.
The clock will be on exhibition at the of
fice of The Call to-day, and it is well
worth seeing.
The Call thanks the gallant crew of
the Monterey. California takes pride in
the ship and the men aboard of her, and
this gift Is appreciated more for what it
indicates even than for its intrinsic
worth, its rarity and Its usefulness.

Men Are Resting Up and Preparing
for the Twenty-Four Hour Con
test, Which Begins on
Friday Night.
The six-day riders are now enjoying
their hard-earned beauty sleep. This
was allowed them In homeopathic doses
â¢of a couple of hours at a time yesterday,
..when they were forcibly awakened and
kept awake for half an hour, and then
â¢permitted to take another snooze. The
trainers say that this is better for them
than a continuous sleep of ten or twelve
â¢hours, the first day after the race.
, None of the men will feel any immediate
.'.effects of the race after to-morrow,
.though what harm may result to
them in the years to come is hard to
foretell. The riders themselves have no
tear, however, and point to "Dutch"
.Waller, who is 44 years of age, and "Old
Man" Ashinger, who is near 50, as evi
dences of longevity in six-day riders.
The men were all up and about by yes
terday noon, apparently much refresheu
by their sleep, and, strange to say, they
ail went over to the Pavilion at different
.times during the day and took a look at
the track where they had tolled so hard.
The interesting ceremony of paying off
the racers took place at noon at the Pa
vilion and every rider personally pre
sented himself at that time except Gimm,
who went to pieces the last two days of
the race.
- The men were paid in the order of their
finish, and Miller came first. He received
$1500 as the first prize and $200 aditional
for breaking the world's record. Doubt
less as Miller's eyes lighted on the glitter
ing pile of golden double eagles he felt
.amply repaid for his week's suffering. His
little wife helped him pack it away, and
will probably also help him spend It.
Alter "cashing In" Miller went back to his
hotel for another short sleep, and says
that except for his knees being sore he is
â as fresh as when he started.
Plucky Oscar- Aronson was the second
man paid, receiving $1000. He, like the
others, had nothing to say save "Thank
you" when he received his money. He
has no wife to spend it on.
Fredericks, who wen third place, drew
down $600, and jolly Teddy Hale, the Irish
champion, received $400. Louis Gimm,
who looked to be the winner up to Thurs
.d'ay morning, got $300 for finishing in fifth
' place. Nawn $200 for sixth, Albert $150 for
â¢seventh and Barnaby $125 for eighth.
Pilkington. Lawson. Julius and Ashinger,
who did not come within the prize list.
were given $10*1 each for having covered
over KOO miles, besides having their ex
penses paid, and were profound in their
expressions of gratification at the liber
ality of the management.
These men will all start on a 24-hour
race next Friday night at 10 o'clock, fin
ishing at the same time Saturday night.
During* such a race, a veritable "sprint"
to these six-day men, they never leave
their wheels once during the ride, for a
dismount or a lost lap means a lost race,
and some Idea of the clip they cut out
can be imagined from the distance cov
ered In the last one in New York, a little
less than 800 miles, when the four leaders
finished in a mile sprint in 1:55 and were
hut inches apart at the tape. It was won
by mm, who has won every 24-hour race
in which he has ever started.
Charles A. Kraft of the Bay City Wheel- i
men, the crack road rider of California,
will start In the 24-hour race, for which I
"he has been training over a month, and j
should give a good account of himself.

will be closed to-night, to give the men ,
; and officials a much-needed rest. It will
reopen to-morrow night and continue open
the rest of the week. The card i
to-morrow night Is a very attractive one, |
Rnd includes a match race between Harry
Elkes, holder of the world's hour record,
and Floyd McFarland, the California
champion, at fifteen miles, paced by ten
tandem teams each. As both are good
followers of pace and like the distance
equally well, the race should be well con
tested. The knowing ones are backing
McFarland, saying that the distance is
lust right for him. but a bit too short
for [kea This is borne out by the state
ment of Elkes' father in The Call the
other day that his boy preferred matches
at from twenty to thirty-three miles. The
Eastern lad will be there all the time,
however, and if "Mac" wins it will only
be after a most gruelling race.
Besides this event there will be ama
teur and professional short-distance races
and the usual exhibitions and record
trials.
' Paced racing at middle distances is a
novelty to California racegoers, to-mor
row night's event being the first of the
kind ever run here.

Poor Louis Gimm at
Death's Door.
PLUCKY RACER A WRECK
UGLY RUMORS OF TRICKERY
ARE WHISPERED ABOUT.
Unfortunate Wheelman Said to Have
Been Drugged to Save Money of
Gamblers Wagered on
His Opponents.
The sir-day bicycle race is a thing- of
the past. The contest of brute physical
endurance has ended, but it has left in
its wake an atmosphere of odium, sug
gestive of trickery and crookedness, that
Is as alarming aa the exhibition was dis
gusting. Louis Gimm, one of the riders,
who for the lirst three days of the race
hid fair to defeat -Miller and capture first
money and the wreath of otoampionship,
now lies at the point of death in his apart
ments at the St. Nicholas Hotel. When
:: totally collapsed about 8 o'clock
â¢in Sunday evening he was carried to his
quarters and later taken to his rooms
in the hotel. Dr. William C. Hopper was
immediately summoned and his first ex
amination, of the rider showed that he
was in a very precarious condition. To all
â¢.ranees he was a complete physical
wreck. Yesterday morning' Dr. Hopper
called in Dr. Clark Burnham in consulta
tion. The two physicians found Gimm's
temperature at 105 decrees, while he is on
the verge of typhoid fever. At present he
is attended by two trained nurses, but the
chances for his recovery are slight. No
one is allowed to see him by the order of
Dr. Hopper.
In speaking of his condition yesterday
Dr. Hopper said Gimm was threatened
with typhoid fever as a result of the race.
He thought his system was in a predis
posed condition when he entered the con
test. In addition to this Gimm has what
id known aa the dilated heart. That is
his heart became so weakened by the de
mands made upon his constitution that it
was unable to pump the blood through
his veins properly and in consequence it
has collected in the valves of the heart.
Dr. Hopper did not hesitate to say that
Gimm's condition was precarious in the
extreme. He admitted that it was the
result of the race.
These are the facts that are generally
known to the public, but yesterday it was
rumored that Gimm had been drugged for
the purpose of clearing the road for Mil
ler, while others assert that if this was
done it was to make the place money
tuny for Aronsun. Dr. Hopper when pre
sented with this view of the case admit
ted that he was going to make an exam
ination of Gimm's condition again for the
purpose of endeavoring to ascertain this
very fact. He said that at present he
could not tell whether Gimm had been
arugged or not, as the symptoms in his
present condition would be similar to a
collapse such as he had suffered.
Very naturally, the managers of the
race, Messrs. Brady and Kennedy hoot
the idea of Gimm having been drugged
and yet Brady says that while the race
was on he discovered a suspicious man in
Gimm's tent, who said that he was a doc
tor. He had placed a cup of coffee in the
tent when discovered. Ttils! man was at
once ejected from the Pavilion by Bradv. I
Brady also declares that he told Leonard,
â¢ iimrn's trainer, that he should move
Gimm from the same tent with Miller.
Instead of doing this Aronson was tak
en Into the tent. At this time Gimm was :
far in the lead. John West, who trained
Miller, also assisted Leonard in handling
Gimm. Every one vouches for the hon
esty of West, who, they declare, is be
yond suspicion. Leonard also speaks In
high terms of West, but he admits that
; he made a mistake in having West assist
: him In handling Gimm.
It was on Wedn< sday afternoon, accord
: ing to Brady, that Gimm suddenly be
â  came deranged, and after that time he
i was never himself and gradually began to
| go to pieces. When the race began Gimm
i was in perfect physical condition, and the
only apparent trouble with which he suf
fered was a badly swollen knee, u^i to the
I time he suddenly denied to lose his rea
son. It is known that Miller was hpavily
backed in New York, while when the race
began Gimm could have been bought at
alluring odds.
Brady speaks of snmc mysterious bet
ting. He declares that he is as anxious
to get to the bottom of any dirty work,
if there was any, as any one. He also
admits that he was surprised by the man
ner in which Gimm suddenly seemed to
become deranged.
It may be possible that If Gimm was
drugged, his food was used as the means
through which to administer the poison.
This Idea is hooted by the bicycle me.i
at the Pavilion, but they admit that it
was possible. It may be that Gimm's col
lapse was due entirely to outraged nature
rebelling against its own abortion, but
this will be definitely proven by tho cx
i amination which is to br made by tlie
doctors. If they discover that the plucky
rider was drugged for th<=- br-fiefit of the
books there may be an investigation and
there may not. If it is necessary to hold
one there is little hope of anything bring

THE SAN FRAXCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, FEBRUARY 21, 1899.
THORPE BODE A
GRAND FINISH
ON TOPMAST
A World's Record
Was Equaled.
SATSUMA BEATEN A HEAD
BUCKWA COULD NOT STAND
THE PACE.
Reopening Day at the Oakland Track.
Three Favorites Won Purses.
Sloan Suspended for a
Ride on Abuse.
There seems to be no difficulty In equal
ing- or breaking records over the Oakland
track with its lightning fast surface. Yes
terday Topmast surprised the large crowd
which attended reopening day by reeling
off one mile and a sixteenth in I:4JR4,
equaling the American record made by
Redskin at Forsyth, Ind., with a catch
on his back. Topmast was ridden by
Charley Thorpe and carried 108 pounds.
Tea to 1 in the betting at post time, he
downed Satsuma in a furious drive. The
latter was best, but Jones displayed poor
tactics, which lost him the race. Old
Buckwa was an odds on choice for this
event, and the crowd fell hard. Pursers
circus horse stood on his hind legs while
at the post and the superstitious ones
raised the limit. He was out of it when
the stretch turn was reached.
Pavoritea divided honors with larger
priced ones in the betting, capturing
three events.
After riding his prettiest for several
moons Cash Sloan once more finds him
self in difficulty. In the fourth number
on the calendar, a six furlong recital,
Cash had the mount on Fred Foster's
corkscrew-legged horse. Abuse. So high
ly was the sprinter thought of that 1 to 4
weis considered a liberal price around post
call. Away none too well at the start,
Sloan found himself in innumerable pock
ets before a quarter had been traversed,
and when he finally extricated himself
later on was downed a head by Tammany
Hall II in 1:13V2- The judges suspended
Sloan pending an investigation of the
race.
Thorpe began proceedings by taking
down the first purse offered for a seven
furlong run, with Stromo, a 4 to 1 chance.
The Stromboli gelding made all the run
ning and won with little to spare from
Una Colorado. Ulm, the favorite, ran un
placed.
Bee Bee. from the Burns & Waterhousc
stable, was touted as a good thing for the
two year old scramble. She was played
down to favoritism and assuming first po
sition in the run home was landed win
ner by a scant margin over Tar Hill, an
outsider. Bamboulia finished third.
The mile selling run proved quite an
easy thing for Torsida. the favorite. Tak
ing the lead from Amelia Fonso when the
stretch was reached Smith's mare was
almost eased up at the wire. Amelia Fon
so disposed of Dare II for the place.
In the final six furlong sprint Wyoming,
the favorite, displayed rare form. He
walked past Eakir.s when the question
was asked, winning cleverly In 1:13%.
Mainstay closed up a deal of ground, lead
i ing Eakins out for second place.
Track Notes.
Seventeen books cut in.
Weaver & Co. disposed of the bay horse
Cabrlllo to Edward Corrlgun yesterday.
Terms private.
There â¢was much criticism over the ef
fort made at the finish by Beauchamp on
Highland Ball. When Beauchamp first
arrived here he brought along a very good
reputation, but If the gossips continue to
talk, he is in danger of losing it.
Coffey's horse Bernardillo openpd favor
ite for the last event on the card, but he
warmed up in such a peculiar manner that,
the judges ordered him scratched. While
being led from the judges' stand to the
paddock, the gelding broke away from
the attendant, making a circuit of the
track at break-neck speed. Then he
turned a somersault over the outsldo
fence opposite the furlong pole, and made
a tour of the stables. Bernardillo is
afflicted at times with so-called "blind
staggers," and in future his entry will not
be received.
The Thornton stake over four miles, will
be the feature of to-morrow's card. The
horses that will probably face the issue
are: David Tenny 115. Mistleton 10?, The
Bachelor 113 and Veloz 113. What-Er
Lou and Buckwa are possible starters. .
Joe Harvey again has possession of
Wheel of Fortune. An understanding was
reached yesterday, and- Hughey Jones,
who claimed the mare out of a selling
race at Ingleside on Saturday last, turned
her over to Harvey yesterday. She will
noi be a starter in the Thornton stake.
The vacation taken by Charley Thorpe
seems to have done him a world of good.
Th>- "old man" piloted three winners with
as much dash and vim as when a young
si.r. His ride <>n Topmast waa a neat
piece of horsemanship.
The Montana horse Tammany Hall
shows great improvement in the hands of
Billy Randall. He ran the race of his
career yesterday.

Mounted Field Practice.
To-morrow the signal corps of the Sec
! ond Brigade, K. G. C, Captain C. L..
Hewes commanding, will parade for
mounted field practice. The officers and
men have been ordered to appear at tho
armory at 8 o'clock, mounted and in cam
paign uniform, with carbines, revolvers
haversacks and canteens, and each man
to provide himself with lunch to carry in
his haversack. The corps will take a ride
through the park, along the beach and
probably come in by way of Ingleside.

President Weinmann Delivers an In
teresting Address Covering Many
Important Fire Insurance
Problems.
The Fire Underwriters' Association of
the Pacific Coast opened' its twenty-third
annual session yesterday with a large at
tendance of members from all over the
coast. Two sessions were held, one In
the morning and the other in the after
noon. At the morning session Calvin
Meade, secretary and treasurer of the as
sociation, submitted a report covering the
Important events and questions that arose
during the past year.

ecutive committee, and Herbert Folger,
chairman of the committee on library and

TAKE ELEVATOR. Hours Bto 5 daily. Evenings 7to 8. Bnndays 9 to 11. , n
classification, also submitted interesting
reports. All of the reports proved the as
sociation to be in a flourishing condition,
financially and otherwise.
Louis Weinmann, president of the asso
ciation, addressed the members on many
interesting matters pertaining to the in
surance business. President Weinmann's
remarks were listened to with unabated
interest. He proved himself to be a very
entertaining talker.
"A spirit of unrest prevails in the un
derwriting business at the present time
and, for that matter, our business has
been invaded by an uncertain quantity
for the past year," said Mr. Weinmann.
"At no period within my knowledge has
there been such an urgent need for the
exercise of sagacity and good judgment
than at the present time. This spirit of
disquiet in nre insurance circles of this
coast has been brought about by inimi
cal legislation, demands for lower rates
and the unreasonable attitude of a cer
tain portion of the press. Through the
ignorance of a part of the press in every
thing that ptrtains to fire insurance, and
the hostilities thus occasioned, much has
been done to give the people the impres
sion that insurance companies are banded
together fo extort excessive premiums
upon small protection.
"The profit line in fire underwriting is
at the present time dangerously near the
margin, and we must educate the peo
ple, not only for the purpose of estab
lishing our business on a sound basis, but
also to make them realize that a profit
means full indemnity to the insured in
every honest loss. San Francisco ha 3
been the most profitable field of insur
ance on the Pacific Coast, chiefly owing
to the. efficiency of the Fire Department.
Their efforts and carefulness have re
duced the number of preventable fires to
a minimum."
In the afternoon the following papers
were read: "Independent Adjusters, E.
P Farnsworth; "The Tribulations of a
Local." J. A. Faymonvllle; "The .Salary
Solicitor System in San Francisco, R. C.
Medcraft; "The Rat Rub," Guy Francis;
"A Brief Analysis of the Theory of De
preciation," H. McDonald Spencer.
The association will elect officers to
serve for the ensuing year at this morn
ing's session. The annual banquet wih
be held this evening at Delmonico s.

His Knife Was Handy.
Matthew Price, a fireman, went Into a
clothing store on Market street, between
Third and Fourth, last night and pur
chased a pair of trousers. When the
clerk prepared to deliver them to him he
claimed they were n.pt the right ones.
Hot words followed and Price exhibited
a knife In a threatening manner. He
â was locked up at the Central Polloo Sta
tion and charged with assault with a
deadly weapon.
CONGREGATIONALISTS CONFER.
Ministers Listen to Interesting
Thoughts by Fellow Clergymen.
.There was a large attendance at the all
day meeting of the Congregational min
isters yesterday. Nearly every repre
sentative of that denomination in this
city and vicinity was seen at headquar
ters in the Young Men's Christian Asso
ciation building.
The programme for the day was car
ried out in its entirety and was greatly
enjoyed by those present. The Rev. C.
R. Brown. D. I)., led the devotional s<-r
--vice, and at its conclusion an able paper
was read by the Rev. I'hilip OÂ«>mbe on
"The Power and Responsibility of th"
Ministry in Civic Regeneration." The p.i
per was a carefully prepared one and
was discussed at length by his brother
ministers.
A splendid repast was laid out in honor
of the day and was done justice to by the
visiting and local pastors. After lunch
Professor R. R. Lloyd reviewed Clark's
"Outlinps of Theology,*" which was fol
lowed by further discussion.
The Rev. J. \V. Phillips, the Rev. McH.
Wallace and the Rev. John P. Koyd gave
their views on "Sermon Plans." which
were discussed and freely criticized. De
votional prayer led by the Rev. W. Yf.
Bcudder closed the afternoon's interesting
meeting.
Luck Is Against Them.
Had It not been for the prompt Inter
ference of Officer CoonÂ«Â»y, F. W. Bryant,
his wife and two children. ag:ed S and 10
years respectively, would have been turn
ed out on the streets last night. The
Bryants have had a run of hard luck.
Mr. Bryant Js an honest, respectable man.
but has bein confined to his home with
illness for Â» long time and the family
in the little house at the rear of 931 Mis
sion street has suffered accordingly. The
rent is overdue and last night the land
lady, Mrs. Chevalier, started to eject
them. Officer Cooney was j'.isiriK at the
time and prevailed on Mrs. Chevalier to
allow the unfortunates to remain over
night in their home. Arrangements for
their proper care will be made to-day.

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, Feb. 20.â The Commercial Ad- !
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The markets here were fairly active and
hard to-day. Americans were firm throughout,

ward 54. It closed 54%.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, Feb. 20.â Wheat was firm to be
gin with to-day and had several good apolo
gies to offer to shorts for being so. The world's
shipments to Europe last week amounted to
only 4,791,000 bushels, or about 2.500.000 bushels
less than Europe's estimated weekly require
ments, while the quantity on ocean passage
was reduced 350,000 bushels, against 290,000
bushels in corresponding week last year. Re
ports from Missouri and Nebraska were that
the winter plant had been seriously injured by
the recent extreme cold. May opened at 72%Â©
7Â£Vic. Liverpool reported wheat stocks in that
port 115,000 bushels larger than last Monday,
while London cable.i claimed only a small busi
ness in wheat and American flour Is a sack
lower since Friday last. This encouraged the
bears to sell, and May declined to 72Vic. The
visible supply, instead of Increasing 500.000.
bushels, as generally expected, showed a de
! crease, of 543,000 bushels, while the Atlantic
port clearances of wheat and flour were large,
amounting to 935,000 bushels. These two an
nouncements sent wheat up to 73% c very quick
ly. Very heavy damage to wheat from late
frosts was claimed from the State of Wash
ington and Oregon. That helped to maintain
the market near the end, and May wheat
I closed at 73 1 8 c.
Large receipts and a poor shipping demand
; weakened corn early, but the decline was all
! recovered and May closed "%c higher. Oats
j followed corn, gaining He -
Provisions were decidedly lopsided and kept
'toppling over every time buyers made an at
; tempt to steady the market for a while. The
enormous receipts of hogs at the yards start
â  ed such heavy selling that the market got
! on a level with stop-loss orders. The stop
! loss orders Increased instead of stopping the
los*. Pork lost 3."Â» c: lard 15c and ribs 16Â®17V4c

THii S'IOCK MARKET.
Mining stocks oppned the week with a fur- |
ther advance, as will be seen by the list of I
sales. Business was good, but the tendency
was downward on the afternoon call. These
stocks are now higher than for some time.
Local securities exhibited no eÃpecial - fea- j
ture. the demand being moderate.
The San Francisco Gas and Electric Company ]
has declared a monthly dividend of 50c per
share, payable March 1.
A dividend of 25c per share will be paid by
the Red Men's Hall Association on the 21st
lnst.
A dividend of 25c per share was paid by the
Hutchinson Plantation Company yesterday.
The Portland mine of Colorado paid a divi
dend of 160,000 yesterday.
The Ferris-Hagerty Copper Mining Company
of Kawlins. Wyo., will pay a dividend of $5000
on March 1.
The Isabella Mining Company of Colorado
will pay a dividend of 6c per share, or $135,000,
on the 25th lnst. This is the mine in which
an Important discover}' of rich gold ore was
reported recently.
Dividends of Utah mines this month have
embraced the following: Bullion, Beck and
Champion, 110,000; Central Eureka, $5000; Grand
Central, $31,250; Silver King, $50,000; Swansea,
$500u.
The Sierra Nevada assessment of 20c per
share will be delinquent in the office to-day.
The official report of General Manager Rosa
of the Comstock Pumping Association Â£or the
week ending February 17 is as follows: Dur
ing the past week the pressure column of the
Rlsdon pumping plant was safely installed,
making a continuous line of 1500 feet of 12
--inch pipe. A slight delay was caused, due to
the placing of an expansion joint In the pipe
line that required special care in bracing and
staying it properly in the shaft. Slight changes
were made in the discharge pipe and a Tew
special connections had to be made at differ
ent points along the line of the column. These
are all finished and the last connections are
being made, so that the line can be tested to
morrow (February IS). For the safety of our
work we have installed an electric line from
our tank connecting with the water company's
flume to our valves in the pressure line on the
1750 level. The line can be extended when re
quired and is so arranged as to work auto
matically. In addition to this device we will
use telephone communication between our tank
and the 1750 station, so that our men in charge
of the elevator can be constantly advised of
the condition of the tank and flume by the
water company's watchman who will be on
duty. Four hundred feet of the Osbiston shaft
connection with the Sutro tunnel has been
cleaned out, making a total of !>4O feet.
M. W. Fox. president, and Joseph R. Ryan,
superintendent, of the Hale & Norcross Mining
Company Inspected the pumping plant as in
stalled and examined the Hale & Norcross
connection with the south lateral branch of the
Sutro tunnpl. with a view of making necessary
repairs to catch up such surface water as Is
now flowing into the lower level. When the re
pairs are made the water will be delivered into
the south lateral of the Sutro tunnel.
In the Sierra Nevada mine the north drift
from the main northwest drift on the 140 level
was advanced 34 feet during the past week and
is now out lrifl feet. The face is in porphyry
and clay, with seams of quartz. On the 900
level of the Union shaft workings the south
drift from the top of the raise at a point 80
feet up is out 71 feet. The face is in clay and
porphyry.
The official report from the Consolidated
California and Virginia mine states that during
the past week the pressure pipe of the Rlsdon
pumping plant wan safely installed In the C.
and C. shaft. The station on the 1400 level
was completed and a second set of chairs put
In working order for this level. The first bulk
head was drained out and Bft feet of the drift
cleaned out, retimbered and track laid, the
intention being to put the level in first-class
repair. Work has been continued on the in
stallation of the electric plant just above the
1750 level. All of the machinery Is in good
order, so that work can be started on the lower
levels as soon as they are drained.
In the Ophir mine, in the old Central tunnel
workings, the north drift toward the Mexican
mine has been extended 10 feet; total length
M feet; face in quartz assaying 80c gold and
1.7G ounces silver. The iron, copper and lead
sulphides that are again showing In the face
of the drift now carry more gold and silver
than at any time since the drift was "started.
On the Sutro tunnel level of the Opnlr the
water-wheel and blower station has been com
pleted and the machinery placed in position.
Connection will be made with the pressure pipe
Hn.l the blower started. As soon as the drift
has been cooled work will be started on the
C Â°ln ne the n Ãe'st & Belcher and Gould & Curry
mines 400 feet of the connecting drift between
the north lateral and Osbiston shaft has been
denned out and track laid, making the total
distance repaired 940 feet. At this point the
shaft was reached and the station found to bÂ©
partly caved and the shaft so tightly Packed
with timbers and debris from the surface that
It is necessary to blast the timbers before they
.â¢an be taken out. This work is now- being
done with three shifts of three men each. Re
timberinK will be started next week. There Â»â 
nulte a stream of water flowing down the shaft,
but it has not been possible to measure the
"Network has been done in the Chollar or
Potosl mines during the past week.

The latest application for nickel steel is
for the construction of racing yachts. A
German firm is to supply the nickel steel
plates for the construction of the Sham
rock, the yacht which is being built to
sail for the America cup. It Is being de
signed by William Fife of Fairlie. and the
work upon it has been begun already.
NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange, la
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality ana
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit thÂ«
office, where complete sets of charts and Ball
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error. If any. Is published
in tht morning papers the following day.
W. S. HUGHES.
Lieutenant. U. S. N.. In charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column, and the successive tides of the.
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
ond the last or right hand column gives the
iÃst tide of the day, except when there are but
three tidee, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

EFFECT OF THE
CENTRAL PACIFIC
REORGANIZATION
Entire System Will Be
Renovated.
MONEY FOR IMPROVEMENTS
HTJNTINGTON WIRES CONGRAT
ULATIONS TO REQUA.
Latter Says New Rolling Stock Has
Been Ordered and the Entire
Southern Pacific System
Will Be Benefited.
Following the settlement of the debt
of the Central Pacific Railroad Company
to the United States is to come an era
:of renewed activity and improvements
! on that line and also on other properties
of the Southern Pacific Company. Many
improvements contemplated of late years
have been held in abeyance awaiting the i
(iisposal of this great financial problem, j
' which has been considered one of the
i most difficult In the history of the road
: and in the career of Coliis P. Hunting
ton.
Owing to the cloud which this incubus
of nearly sixty million dollars of debt cast
on the credit of the Southern Pacific
Company, it was found impracticable for
i that corporation to go into the money
markets of the world to raise funds for
much-needed improvements. This ob
| stacle has been removed by the settle
ment announced last week, and a scheme
! of financiering is now in contemplation,
' which will not only provide ample mtans
1 for liquidating the entire funded and fluitt
ing indebtedness of the Central Pacific,
i bui will leave an ample surplus tor im
provements to the roadbed, additions to
I the rolling stock and even for extensions
i of the line.
I That President Huntlngton of the
i Southern Pacific Company is extremely
' gratified over the outcome of his long
' pending controversy with the Government
, is shown by a telegram received yesterday
i from New York by President Isaac JL.
Requa of the Central Pacific Railroad,
; signed by C. P. Huniington, which read
as follows:
"1 congratulate you and the majority
of the board of directors on the consum
mation of the result we have so long
hoped and worked for, namely, the settle
ment or the Central Pacific Railroad
("ompaily'B debt to the Government. My
associates and myself want you to under
stand that we appreciate all that you and
! they have done toward this result, and
! are glad to give you all the credit you
deserve for your assistance in behalf of
the stockholders. 1 am confident that
the future will show that those who have
stood by each other in defense of the
best interests of the Central Pacific Rail
road Company and its shareholders build
ed better than they knew."
President Requa was no less pleased
over the outcome, and said he believed
the result of the settlement would soon
be visible in the improvements that
would be inaugurated and in those under
way that would now be rushed to com
pletion. Referring to the closing of the
gap on the coast division between Surf
and Elwood he said:
"The company is now very well pre
pared for that work. It has been busy
during the past year straightening out
the crooks and grades on that line, and
â¢will now have sufficient money at its com
mand to connect the two ends of the line.
The work of doing so will no doubt, be
immediately begun. I calculate it will
take about twelve months to have it in
shape A great deal of work has already
been done on the main line as far as Sa
linas in the way of putting tne line into
condition for attaining a greater rate of
speed with safety.
"ThÂ« question of the Central Pacific s
debt to the Government," he continued,
i "is now out of the way. for good. You
will hear no more talk about the Central
Pacific Railroad being owned by the Gov
ernment. Under the terms of the settle
ment we shall pay the Government $15,
--1 000 000 on the 7th of July. After that they
will get about $3,000,000 every six months
' until the balance is paid. The notes to
1 the Government bear interest at 3 per
cent and are twenty in number, the total
amount of the debt being something over
$55,000,000.
"In order to raise the money to meet
these notes the entire Central Pacific sys
tem comprising about fourteen hundred
mile's ol railroad, will be bonded for $100,
--000 000 the bonds to bear 4 per cent. The
Government aided line only takes in
about 800 miles. Before these new bonds
i ure issued all the 5 per cent bonds in the
! hands of the holder? of the first mort
gage bonds will be called in and replaced
on satisfactory terms with the new bonds.
This will be taken up at the annual meet
ing of the stockholders, which will be
held in San Francisco in April.
"I look forward to a very active and
profitable business awakening on this
coast fr'ta one end to the other. In anti
cipation of what has come to pass the

PROVIDES FOR BOND ISSUE.
Financial Details of the Central Pa
cific Reorganization Plan.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Feb. 20.â The Central
Pacific reorganization plan provides for
an issue of $100,000,000 forty-five-year
first mortgage refunding fours, $25,000,
--000 thirty-year three and one-halfs, $20.
--000,000 4 per cent cumulative preferred
and $67,275,000 common stock. The dis
tribution of securities is as follows:
Of the $100,000,000 fours the United
States retains $47,056,000 as collateral
for the 3 per cent notes, $51,235,500 al
lowed for exchange of existing bonds
and the syndicate purchas.es $1,690,500
to provide for cash requirements.
The 3Vfes are secured by a pledge on
sinking fund securities and land grants;
$13,695,000 to take up other bonds, while
the syndicate retains the remainder.
The $20,000,000 preferred stock is de
livered to the Southern Pacific Com
pany in consideration of the delivery
by that company of an equal amount
of Its four per cent bonds. The syndi
cate takes all of the common stock and
is offered to shareholders on the pay
ment of $2 per share. The fixed charges
of the new company are estimated at
$4,875,000. The average net earnings of
the Central Pacific for the past ten
years was $4,893,259, which has been in
creased to $5,582,939 by income from
sinking fund .securities.
It is expected that $4,500,000 will be
provided to finance the plan. Out
standing bonds of $1000 each will be ex
changed as follows: One thousand dol
lar bonds of the Southern Pacific Cali
fornia first series "A" receive $33 33
cash, $1000 in new fours; $50 in 3ViB,
series "B," "C," "D," "E." "F," "G,"
"H" and "I," get $29 17 cash, $1000 in
4s and $50 in 3V,s. Western Pacific
"A" and "B" firsts, $35 cash. $1000 in 4s
and $50 in 3M>s; San Joaquin Valley
firsts, $r.O cash, $1000 in 4s and $75 in
3 l-2s; Southern Pacific land bonds,
$41 67 cash, $500 in 4s and $700 in ?. l ~x;
ditto 6 per cent bonds, $50 cash, $500 in
4s and $900 in SJ^B] ditto I per cent
bonds. $41 67 cash, $500 in 4s, $800 in 3s;
California and Oregon first "A" and
"B," $29 15 cash, $100 in 4s and $200 in
3%5.
PACIFIC ROADS OBLIGATIONS.
Governent Fares Well in Securing a
Settlement of Its Claims.
WASHINGTON, Feb. 20.â A siateim-nt
concerning the settlement of the Pacific
railroad obligations way made to-day by
the Department of Justice from which
the following is an extract:
"It will be remembered that by the bill
authorizing the construction of the Pacific
railroads, passed in 18(52, the United States
agreed to lend its credit to the various
Pacific railroad companies for the pur
pose of aiding them in the construction of
a line of railway from the Missouri River
to the Pacific Ocean. For that purpose
bonds were issued in aid of the Union
Pacific Railroad to the amount of $27,
--236,512; to what was subsequently known
as the Kansas Pacific to the amount of
$6,303,000; to the Central Pacific to the
amount of $25,885,120; and to the Western
Pacific to the amount of $1,970,560.
"Upon these bonds without direct re
imbursement the United States paid in
terest from the date of their respective
issues, so that in IRO7 there was due to the
United States from the Union Pacific
Railroad Company the sum of $58,488,223;
from the Kansas Pacific in round num
bers the sum of $13,000,000. On the Ist of
February, 1899. there was due from the
Central Pacific Railroad Company the
sum of $53,389,062, and there was due from
the Western Pacific on the same date the
sum of $5,423,6Â«52.
"The vast accumulation of principal
and interest due to the United States
from these companies has heretofore
given great concern to the Government,
it having been the common Impression
that the United States would never re
ceive anything like the full amount of its
investment by way of return. Various
propositions have been advanced from the
most conservative financiers for the ad
justment of these claims, ranging all the
way from propositions to settle for 50
cents on the dollar to refunding measures
which were intended to extend the pay
ment for 100 years.
"The agreement just made with the
Central Pacific Railroad Company as
sures beyond the slightest doubt the full
payment of the debt of the Central Pa
cific and the Western Pacific railroads,
aggregating $58,812,715 48. This debt by
the agreement is funded for payment
within the period of ten years; one-twen
tieth thereof being payable each six
months during that period, and the Gov
ernment receiving 3 per cent interest
upon thÂ» balance remaining due from
time to time. The treasury will imme
diately receive about $12,000,000 by the sal.?
of the first four notes maturing under this
agreement, which are taken by Speyer &
Co. as an earnest of good faith and as a
reliance which they place upon the abil
ity of the contracting parties to meet the
obligation entered into by the agreement.
"Thf total result of all these transac
tions is that out of these claims which it
was once supposed would not realise the
Government 50 cents on the dollar the
United States will receive the enormous

"SIGH OF THE
CROSS" IS A
BRUTAL PLAY
Pruriency in Religious
Mask.
WAR DRAMA AT THE ALCAZAR
CLEVER OPERA SINGERS IN
VAUDEVILLE.
"Madeleine" Crowds the Tivoli.
Black Patti's Coons Still at the
California â Other Current
Attractions.
Thrift is the watchword in modern
theatricals. Thrift is a power against
which even the most sacred traditions
and superstitions are not invincible.
Out at the Chutes the Passion Play is
presented by a projecting machine, to
say nothing of an amateur ballet and
a dwarfed paralytic in the life. I have
dropped a nickel in the slot and heard
what were alleged to be the maniacal
ravings of John McCollough. At the
Columbia Theater last night I saw a
so-called religious drama, written by
Wilson Barrett, and entitled "The Sign
of the Cross."
If ever theatrical man had thrift Mr.
Barrett has itâ and audacity, too. Ho
has rushed in where angels would have
feared to tread, and prospered opulent
ly. Himself a man of few talents, he
has yet been clever enough to trade
on the weaknesses of others. "The
Sign of the Cross" has made him rich.
It is a goose-flesh drama to frighten
women and children, to pander with
hypocritical pruriency to the baser in
stincts of men, to seduce simple-mind
ed ministers of the gospel into writing
tearful testimonials, thereby advertis
ing the play, and, more than incident
ally, themselves. Mr. Barrett is su
premely thrifty, and I have no doubt
that his representatives will do a rous
ing business on Powell street at the
sign of the cross. The cross was our
in bold relief last night on Powell
street. It was hung high and flaring
and made of crimson incandescents.
and a big crowd passed under it and
into the Columbia Theater to do lust
what Mr. Barrett expected them to 3u.
Preachers, I was told, were there by
the dozen, and Mr. Greenway and other
worldly gentlemen sat expectantly In
the front row. Women wept, a child
or two cried, men and ushers applaud
ed; the curtain was run up again and
again after each act. In the parlance
of Mr. Barrett's profession, the play
was a hit. A vulgar, immoderate play,
that to the seriously holy ridicules all
that is holy, that brutalizes one of the
most thrilling of all religious legends,
that makes a vaudeville act of physical
torture and a showman's virtue of a
scene in which a girl is almost openly
ravished by a wine and passion-drunk
manâ and the play is a hit.' We San
Franciscans are prone to sneer at the
theatrical tastes of New York. I have
done some sneering In that direction
on my own account. But be it everlast
Office: S. W. Cor. Post and Powell Streets. Telepbone:
Opposite Union Square. Main 5713.

ingly written to the credit of gay New-
York that she refused to accept this
Neronian nightmare Which Mr. Wilson
Barrett is pleased to call "The Sign of
the Cross." And unless I am greatly in
error the horrible torture scene which
we were treated to last night was omit
ted in the New York production. In
this scene a boy, or, to be accurate, a
woman clad as a, boy, is flogged until
she falls shrieking to the floor and then
taken to another apartment, from
which are heard the straining of the
rack and the ululations of the victim.
The assault scene I refuse to adver
tise by describing. It sickened me, and
my stomach is not unaccustomed to
hard knocks. Let the Orpheum dis
close an act like this one and the po
lice would raid the place. Mr. Barrett,
like Mr. Hall Came and other thrifty
sensationalists who have profited by
the indorsement of a sensational clergy,
garbs his fleshliness in the spacious
overcoat of "religion." Marcus Super
bus, Prefect of Rome, desires the body
of the Christian girl Merciaâ and this
is given you not in narrative, not in
insinuation, but in brutal action. Fail
ing in all other things, Marcus Super
bus finally gets religion, and together
they enter the arena to die with the
rest of Nero's Christian victims. That's
where the morals come in. Morals for
sooth.
So far as the actors and scenic equip
ment go liberal praise is due. Charles
Dalton, who plays the Prefect, is a
heroic actor of magnetism and art: Miss
Agnes Scott is an admirable study in
the part of Berenis, the jealous pa
trician; and iiiss Irene Rooke is pa
thetically adequate to the heroine. In
deed there are many clever players in
this English company, and the pictorial
and actorial arrangement of the stage
is excellent. But the play is profane,
inartistic, brutal and illiterateâ a dank,
depressing melodrama. If any of the ]
gentlemen of the cloth intend writing
or preaching about it I hope they will
not overlook the Passion Play and the
paralyzed party and the amateur ballet
out at the Chutes. ââââ
ASHTON STEVENS.
California.
Black Patti's Troubadours entered the
second week of their engagement last
night to a good house.
Treasurer Louis A. Morgenstern of the
California Theater is to be tendered a
grand complimentary benefit at that
houae on Friday evening, when the Black
Pattl Troubadours will give some special
features. The popular treasurer will
have a rousing benefit.
Nance O'Neil Is to come back for eight
performances, beginning next Monday
night. A petition has been signed by
many of the leading people of the State
requesting a return engagement prior to
her departure for London, and in conse
quence a week has b<^en securt-d at the i
California Theater. The opening play is
to be "Magda." which will be repeated
on Thursday night. On Tuesday evening
Mi?e O'Neil will appear for the first time
here in the new "East Lynne." Other
plays for the week are "Ineomar. "I he
Jewess, " "Oliver Twist" and â¢â¢Camille.
Alcazar.
The stock company at the Alcazar j
scored a genuine success last night in ;
Belasco and Fyles' military drama, "The
Girl 1 Left Behind Me." The theater was j
crowded and the audience enthusiastic.
At the close of the third act. when the re
lief of the apparently doomed men and
women at Post Kennion is accomplished, j
the audience went wild and the curtain j
had to be raised six times. Ernest i
Hastings as Lieutenant Edgar llawkes- :
worth was manly and impressive and I
made the hit of the evening. Clarence j
Montalne as Major Burleigh and Master
Jimmy Horn as his son Dick were excel- I
lent, and George Osbourno as General \
Kennion and George Webster as Lieu- ,
tenant Morton Parlow, the villian were
praiseworthy. Misa Adelaide Fitzallen :
as Kato Kennion and Miss Juliet Crosby
as Lucky Hawkesworth were charming
and Miss Laura Crews, a product of the
Northwest, was ingenuous. The other
parts were well played.
Orpheum.
The most artistic of the new acts at
the Orpheum this week is contributed by
J. K. Murray and Clara Lane, who ap
pear in costume and sing several selec
tions from the light operas that have
been, threaded around a sufficient little
plot. Miss Lane has an agile and sym
pathetic soprano and Mr. Murray a bary
tone of charming quality. Their ringing
iÃ refreshingly legitimate; in fact, the en
tire act is a graceful addition to vaude
ville. A quite different turn of a noisy,
rough-and-tumble nature is furnished by
Lew Bloom i.nd Jane Cooper, under the !
title of "A Picture From Life. " Mr.
Bloom is an inebriated tramp and Miss
Cooper is first a loud-voiced city girl and
later a village spinster. In lines, action
and performance the skit is incline! to
roughness, but there is enough fun in it
to make people laugh in spit** of their
better naturesâ indeed, the audience at
the opening performance could not get
enough of it. Mips Edith Crask, a heavy
weight but nimble premiere danseuse,
does a number of national dances in coh
tume and adds to the gayety of the crowd
by announcing them in voice and accent
that hardly belong to this world.
Of the hold-over turns the best are Car
ter de Haven and Bonnie Male, juvenile
cake-walkers: Shortis and his fretless
banjo, and the dog and monkey troupe.
Conroy and McDonald and Lang and
Keeler have changed their acts, but they
are still something less than the Orpheum
standard.
Tivoli.
B'ne B'rith held carnival in the Tivoli
last night, where "Madeleine, or the Magic
Kiss," was presented to a crowded house.
The opera is one of many charms. It is
given a strong production and will run
until further notice.
Olympia.
The new feature of the bill at the
Olympia this week is Sherman and Mor
risey, acrobats and comedians. They
scored a great hit in their comedy work.
Querita Vincent was welcomed back and
seems to be more popular than ever. The
one Lynwood is still a card. The bill has
some ten or more other good acts.
Chutes.
Violet Dale sang badly, bucked and
winged cleverly and finally summer
saulted herself into two encores at the
Chutes Free Theater last evening. She
is the best acrobatic dancer in
San Francisco. Jack Symonds mono
logued for half an hour and kept the
large audience roaring. He is genuinely
funuy. The Morrelle nanjoed. told stories
and made a hit. The three De Bolien
brothers repeated tjheir acrobatic success
of last week and Pianka continued to feed
her lions strips of raw meat from her lips.
The Passion Play was appreciated. To^
morrow afternoon. Washington's birth
day. Emil Markeberg will make a balloon
ascension, and on Thursday night the am
ateur ballet will be repeated.
Central Park.
The "gravity horses" at the Steeple
chase at Central Park have caught the
popular fancy and thousands of persons
of all ages are enjoying the sport. The
Buccess of the Steeplechase in the East
and in Europe seems destined to be dupli
cated on this coast.

ANSWERS CHARGES
Not Guilty of "Repre
v- hensible Conduct."

.;. :. TO SANTIAGO.
Draws a Contrast Between the Be- â 
ports of Sampson and Himself
â¢" . After the Destruction of
:â ' Cervera's Fleet.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Feb. 20.â Rear Admiral j
. W. S. Schley, having been granted per- j
. mission to answer the charges recently j
"â¢.made against him In a communication .
.'.sent to the Senate, to-day handed to the
Committee on Naval Affairs his state
â  ment. In which he disclaims any purpose
of controversy with the Navy Depart
ment. The letter of the department, he
â  Bays, so far as it relates to him, can be ;
'summarized under the following heads:
"The delay off Cienfuegos; that alleged
plow progress toward Santiago de Cuba
from Cienfuegos; the retrogade move
ments of the 26th and 2Ttli of May; the
battle of Santiago and the destruction of
- Cervei fleet."
The admiral says his orders from
Sampson on May 19 were to blockade
Cienfvfegos. which he proceeded to do. He
had no knowledge that there were any
insurgents about Cienfuegos who were
friendly until the Marblehead arrived on
the -24th. Then he sent Commander Mc-
Calla of the Marblehead ashore to estab
lish relations with the insurgents, the re-
BUlt or which was made known to him at j
3:40 p. m. on May 24. Then, for the first
time, he learned definitely that the Span
' ish fleet was not at Cienfuegos. Within;
two hours be started for Santiago. >% ;
"What possible ground for criticism, i
he says, "adverse to me there can be in
all this I do not see. 1 was on the spot. ;
. acting under orders which cave me en- I
tire discretion, and yet clothed me with
the responsibility of going to Santiago
only after 1 was satisfied that the fleet
was not at Cienfuegos."
lie quotes from the dispatches of
' Sampson of May 20, in which the latter j
said Schley should "hold his fleet off
fuegos. If the Spanish ships have
: put into Santigao they must come either
-. to Havana or to Cienfuegos to deliver
the munitions which they are said to
â¢ bring for use in Cuba.
â¢ â¢I am therefore of the opinion that the I
best chance to capture these ships will!
be to hold the two points, Cienfuegos and
Havana, with all the force we can mus
ter. If later it should develop that these
' vessels are at Santiago we can then as- j
semble off that port the snips best Suited ;
for the purpose and completely blockade I
it. Until we then receive more positive
information we shall continue to hold Ha
â  vana and Cienfuegos."
Admiral Schley .-aid: "This letter is not
printed either in the appendix or execu- i
tive' document C. but 1 received it on the
; 23d. and it shows that I was expected to
hold my squadron off Cienfuegos." ;
As to the slow progress toward San
tiago on account of the Eagle, he states
â . that she was a part of the force which
Admiral Sampson had seen fit to send
him. and he had no right to abandon
her. The weather also was rough and not
much better speed could have been kept
up. He had been ordered to proceed
:"â¢ "cautiously." If, however, he had known
that the Spanish Heel was in Santiago
â¢ â no consideration of the Eagle or any one
â¢ efse would have prevented him from get
. ting there at the earliest possible mo
.:ment with the larger vessels, but the
â¢â weather and sea made it Impossible to do
â¢better than he did. Speaking of the. "re
' trograde movements'.' which the Secre
tary, adapting the language of Admiral
'Sampson, characterizes as "reprehensible
.'.conduct," Admiral Schley says that in
"forty-two years' service "never was such :
language used to characterize conduct of
mine, and 1 see no reason for it now."
"â 'He adds: "Acting in accordance with
''my. best judgment, in view of the circum- '

emy's fleet in Santiago harbor."
: - As" to the battle of Santiago Admiral
.Schley says the facts of that contest
. speak for themselves. He quotes from

. squadron and said the "victory seems big

cism of my course prior to the discovery !

â¢ has ever been made by either of them to

although more than live weeks had passed

roy conduct of the flying squadron."
